[{"id":40793,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/time-now-budding-philadelphia-76ers-miami-heat-fades-away","date":"2018-04-22","title":"These Philadelphia 76ers are blossoming into a title contender right before our eyes","content":" MIAMI \u2014 Back in 2014, when the Miami Heat were wrapping up their championship-fueled era, the Philadelphia 76ers began plotting their own. And they did it unconventionally, laughably and by any measure, dreadfully. It was Year One of the most ambitious rebuilding plan before or since, when the Sixers willingly laid down and became a doormat and allowed other teams to wipe their sneakers on them. That season, while LeBron James and Dwyane Wade and Chris Bosh cruised to a fourth straight appearance , and their last together, in the NBA Finals, the Sixers lost 63 games . And then they got better at this tanking technique and lost 64 and 72 the next two years. But fast-forward to now, to Saturday at American Airlines Arena, and the roles with the Heat and Sixers are threatening to flip. Maybe not so drastically, but it\u2019s clear through four games of this first-round playoff series that the Sixers are going one way and the Heat another. The Sixers have Ben Simmons and Joel Embiid, a pair of young bedrocks slowly building something with the potential to be big. The Heat? They have banners in the rafters commemorating what they used to be, not so long ago. Philly also has something else on Miami, namely a 3-1 series lead after Simmons became the first rookie since Magic Johnson to drop a triple-double in a playoff game and Embiid fought through a poor shooting game and an irritating protective mask to spook any Heat player that challenged him at the rim. It was the Sixers who made all the right plays in the final crucial moments in the 106-102 win , getting key stops and buckets and pulling away, a team with a young core turning mature, and doing it rapidly, despite their lack of post-season experience. And having a front-row seat to this new Process was none other than Wade, a proud if aging member of the extinct Big Three who realizes something unique is happening with the Sixers. \u201cThis is a very good team,\u201d said Wade. \u201cThey\u2019ve got talent at almost every position. This is definitely one of the best first-round opponents I\u2019ve played in my career.\u201d Are the Sixers all that, already? \u201cThey\u2019re good,\u201d said Wade. \u201cThey\u2019re special. They put the right team together.\u201d Yes, they have. Maybe it wasn\u2019t properly done in the spirit of competition, and perhaps they embarrassed themselves if not the league while doing so, but that\u2019s all behind the Sixers right now. What\u2019s ahead of them is a potential series-clinching Game 5 in Philly and from there, who knows? Yes, the core of the Sixers is Simmons, Embiid and Dario Saric, all under 25, and in the playing rotation only JJ Redick and Marco Belinelli earned any significant playoff money. But if a young team is ever going to reach the NBA Finals, this is the right time, and this is the right team. Just look at the wide-open landscape in the East: LeBron and the Cavaliers, winners of the last three East titles, are down 2-1 to the Pacers and haven\u2019t appeared this fragile since LeBron returned to Cleveland. The Celtics are missing Kyrie Irving and Gordon Hayward. Toronto is the No. 1 seed in the East but inspires few outside Canada. Why not the Sixers? Why not now? Simmons is lacking a jump shot and little else, and still manages to score anyway. His direction of the club in the fourth quarter of Game 4 was near-masterful; Simmons stayed poised, found the open man and popped the Heat\u2019s comeback hopes with an uncontested dunk when Miami pulled within a point. Embiid couldn\u2019t hit a shot and yet didn\u2019t fall into a funk; rather he terrorized Miami by being a defensive force, punctuated by his spike of a Goran Dragic late-fourth quarter breakaway layup attempt (followed by an Embiid stare down). \u201cThey make you pay every time you make a mistake,\u201d said Wade. Speaking of which, the Sixers had 27 turnovers, certainly the recipe for disaster, and still found a way. In the words of coach Brett Brown: \u201cI\u2019m surprised we won this game. We really didn\u2019t have any right to win this game.\u201d But maybe it\u2019s just additional proof that this is Philly\u2019s time. It\u2019s quite a contrast to the ex-bully on the block. Four years after LeBron made the second biggest decision of his life, the Heat are still searching for the identity they had when the champagne flowed, and the party rolled on South Beach. The only reminder is Wade, and at age 36 he\u2019s only capable of having flashes now, like his 28 points in Game 2 and an impressive 25-point follow up Saturday that was marred only by a missed free throw in the final seconds. Besides that, there\u2019s nothing special. Pat Riley\u2019s latest attempt to recreate a winner is looking dubious right now. Riley decided two summers ago to build the Heat around a seven-foot center with low post-skills, which means Riley gave a $100 million to a dinosaur. And one with a decaying relationship with coach Erik Spoelstra. Hassan Whiteside can\u2019t get on the floor in today\u2019s NBA, where small-ball makes him a liability in certain situations. With no shooting range, and perhaps no incentive to develop one, Whiteside finds himself on the bench in fourth quarters and on the nerves of Spoelstra. \u201cHe\u2019s a prisoner of the style of play,\u201d said Brown. Plus: Riley also paid Josh Richardson, James Johnson, Tyler Johnson and Kelly Olynyk. Which means the Heat are almost guaranteed to be a 43-win team fighting for the final playoff spot for the next few years. When the Heat searched for someone to bail them out Saturday, who did they turn to? An aging All-Star who\u2019s on the downside, which says something about Wade \u2026 and the Heat\u2019s roster. \u201cHe ended up being our best option,\u201d said Spoelstra. There\u2019s another path the Heat can take, of course. They could follow the current Hawks, Nets, Lakers and Magic, who all took their cues from the 2014 Sixers, and take a few steps back before moving forward. But that\u2019s not a fool-proof plan \u2014 have you seen the Magic the last few years? \u2014 and besides, losing by any means isn\u2019t in Riley\u2019s DNA. So, mediocrity it is, then. Meanwhile, the Sixers have Embiid and Simmons and if you ask fans in Philly, they\u2019d say it was well worth the steep price, in terms of the misery of tanking, paid for them. \u201cThey\u2019re two players that have the chance to be great,\u201d said Brown. \u201cJoel has no right to be doing some of the things he does. Ben\u2019s composure down the stretch is amazing. Those two are exceptional.\u201d What the Sixers just did was win a pair in Miami, under the banners that hung over them, was fly in the face of basketball convention which says youth doesn\u2019t get served in the post-season. They can close out at home and then get the survivor of Celtics-Bucks, and Philly can expect to be the favorite in that conference semifinal. \u201cI can see how much we\u2019ve grown and how much more room we have to grow,\u201d said Brown. \u201cTo come here and get a win, in this building, against an organization of winning and culture and history, it\u2019s special.\u201d There\u2019s another story here: If the Sixers eliminate the Heat, then it could be curtains for Wade, who doesn\u2019t have a contract for next season, who hasn\u2019t committed to playing beyond this season, and who paused suspiciously for about three seconds when asked if Saturday was his final game in Miami. \u201cI don\u2019t want to answer that right now,\u201d he said. Whether he sticks around or takes the sunset cruise, Wade must realize that a transformation is taking place in the East. After years of deliberately bad basketball the Sixers are finally bearing fruit, and oh, speaking of food, Wade and the Heat can chew on this for a minute: The Sixers have room under the salary cap to give Embiid and Simmons some help next season. LeBron James, free agent-to-be, might reach the conclusion that the Sixers are his best championship option. Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. You can e-mail him here , find  his archive here and follow him on Twitter . The NBA\u2019s early-entry deadline for candidates is Sunday night."},{"id":40795,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/thon-maker-matthew-dellavedova-fuel-game-3-win","date":"2018-04-21","title":"Milwaukee Bucks get more from role players in crucial Game 3 win over Boston Celtics","content":"MILWAUKEE \u2013 All the ingredients were there for the Milwaukee Bucks, down 0-2 to the Boston Celtics, to claw their way back into the teams\u2019 first-round Eastern Conference series . A hungry, boisterous Friday night crowd at the BMO Harris Bradley Center. A somewhat cranky Bucks squad, unhappy both with the two performances they gave in Boston and with the heat that came their way since. A couple essential members of the Milwaukee rotation \u2013 point guard Eric Bledsoe and forward Jabari Parker \u2013 who, with bold talk, put themselves squarely under the bright and rather, hot put-up-or-shut-up spotlight. And of course, Thon Maker. Wait, who? It\u2019s an NBA clich\u00e9 by now that role players and reserves are most likely to help their teams in the playoffs at home rather than on the road. It\u2019s all about comfort zones, intimidation factors and other elements that either soothe or fray a young or fringe player\u2019s nerves. Well, Maker was a poster guy for that in the Bucks\u2019 116-92 victory over the Celtics. He scored 14 points, hit three of his four 3-point attempts, grabbed five rebounds and blocked five shots in 24-plus impactful minutes off the bench. Compare that to Maker\u2019s DNP-CD in Game 1 and his lone scoreless minute in Game 2, both held on the parquet at Boston\u2019s TD Garden. Maker and, for that matter, backup Bucks guard Matthew Dellavedova contributed mightily, the former at both ends, the latter defensively as he dogged Boston ball handlers for 94 feet. They brought energy, tenacity and determination that somehow made it into neither their carry-on bags or checked luggage for the initial trip to New England. \u201cThey both came ready to play,\u201d Bucks All-Star Giannis Antetokounmpo said, \u201cand checked into the game and set an example for the team. And everybody else followed.\u201d Maker, the 7-foot-1 second-year project from the South Sudan, got minutes in relief of big man Tyler Zeller, who started because of John Henson\u2019s aching back. Dellavedova was in on a need basis, dictated by the damage being done through two games by Boston point guard Terry Rozier. Neither of them had done much this season to either crow about or suggest helpful playoff contributions. Dellavedova, hobbled by tendinitis and an ankle sprain, played in only 38 games and dipped from 7.6 points per game in 2016-17 to 4.3 this season. Maker regressed more on merit. After starting 34 games as a rookie, he was called on to do so just 12 times this season. His playing time went up from 9.9 to 16.7 minutes but his production backslid from 14.5 points and 7.3 rebounds pro-rated to 36 minutes to 10.4 and 6.5. In short, Maker did little to justify mentor Kevin Garnett\u2019s bold prediction at the season\u2019s onset that the lanky defender with the pterodactyl wingspan one day would be named the NBA\u2019s Most Valuable Player. Most Disappointing Sophomore was more like it. None of which mattered a bit Friday. \u201cSometimes with guys that don\u2019t do it every night,\u201d Boston coach Brad Stevens said, \u201cyou\u2019re more fearful of what they\u2019re capable of than what their averages are. That was the case tonight.\u201d One thing about the playoffs is, a big game or even a game-turning moment can wash away a regular season\u2019s worth of heartbreak or ineffectiveness. Maker was feeling amped before the series even began, but it wasn\u2019t until the scene shifted to Milwaukee that he showed it. \u201cPlayoffs just really get me hyped,\u201d Maker said after his dynamic, rim-denying, plus-23 effort. \u201cThe crowd, even if it\u2019s an away crowd, just to hear that noise, you just get motivated. You just feel like you have to leave everything on the floor. \u201cIt\u2019s the end of the season. There\u2019s teams sitting at home watching us, and I do not want to be those guys. So I got to find a way to get out there and give everything I got. I have nothing to lose, like I said. I really love this game, and the playoffs are the biggest stage. So I gotta find a way to impact the game.\u201d To put Maker\u2019s shots and swats in context, he had six games all season before Friday in which he played as many minutes. Two in which he scored more points. One in which he posted a better plus\/minus rating. And none in which he blocked so many shots. Four of his five blocks Friday came in the first quarter. \u201cThon for me is like a little brother,\u201d Antetokounmpo said. \u201cWe had a conversation two weeks ago and I told him he has to bring that killer mentality back.\u201d Maker, you might recall, played well at a similar point last postseason, scoring 11 points in Milwaukee\u2019s 104-77 Game 3 blowout of the Raptors at home. That, too, was a spirited game, one that put the Bucks up 2-1 in the series and looked like a tipping point \u2026 until Toronto ran off three consecutive victories. The challenge to the Bucks now is even greater. Barely 36 hours after fending off, decisively, a potential 0-3 hole, they will need to do it all again on Sunday (1 ET, ABC). That means Khris Middleton, who topped Milwaukee in points (23), rebounds (eight) and assists (seven), leading the way again. With Antetokounmpo bedeviling Boston\u2019s defense and maybe avoiding the foul trouble that limited his playing time to 27:21. It means Bledsoe, with 17 points and four assists, staying on the right side of his matchup with Rozier, the Celtics point guard he had dissed with a \u201cWho\u2019s that?\u201d remark after Game 2. It means Parker backing up his grumbles about short minutes by contributing something akin to the 17 points with five boards, two assists and two blocks he had in 30:13 Friday. Parker, a restricted free agent this summer whose relationship with Bucks management seems more than a little strained, declined to talk with reporters afterward. That instantly did his points-to-comments ratio some good. And then, if Milwaukee gets all that or something close to it in Game 4, it will hope that someone, anyone, can offer the boosts Dellavedova and Maker brought in this one. So when he says that the Pelicans had the league\u2019s most powerful trio this season before DeMarcus Cousins suffered a season-ending Achilles injury, it should\u2019ve had you pause. The Pelicans might have the best Big 3 of this postseason with the way Rondo, All-Star big man Anthony Davis and Jrue Holiday have played through the first three games of their staggering first-round playoff series against the Portland Trail Blazers . The Pelicans have a chance to finish the sweep in Game 4 today ( 5 p.m. ET, TNT ). Like everyone else, Rondo is left to wonder what might have been if Cousins was still available. Will Guillory of Nola.com has more : The Pelicans came into the season with a roster built around the NBA\u2019s most overpowering frontcourt combination \u2014 Antony Davis and Cousins \u2014 and arguably one of the league\u2019s best two-way players in guard Jrue Holiday. The trio was still figuring out how to work together after Cousins was traded to New Orleans during All-Star weekend last season, but they showed flashes of greatness before the big man suffered a ruptured lest Achilles\u2019 tendon that brought his season to an end. Veteran point guard Rajon Rondo said at Friday\u2019s practice that before Cousins\u2019 injury the Pelicans had the NBA\u2019s best Big 3, and their potential was the main reason he decided to sign a one-year deal with New Orleans last summer. \u201cThat was my biggest reason why I wanted to come here,\u201d Rondo said. \u201cObviously, (Cousins) went down, but the weapons they had before I got here with the Big 3 \u2014 Jrue, A.D. and Cuz \u2014 I thought they were the best Big 3 in the league, by far. Hands down, we had the best Big 3.\u201d"},{"id":47207,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/inconsistent-washington-wizards-play-potential-game-3","date":"2018-04-21","title":"Inconsistent Washington Wizards play up to potential in Game 3","content":"WASHINGTON \u2013  The playoffs , with their small sample sizes and with bad matchups sometimes outweighing overall advantages, can yield weird results. Sometimes, the team that wins a record 73 games in the regular season blows a 3-1 lead in The Finals. And assuming that the New Orleans Pelicans don\u2019t blow a 3-0 lead in their series with the Portland Trail Blazers, this will be the 12th straight year in which a team with home-court advantage has lost in the first round. Along those lines, the Washington Wizards trailed 2-0 entering Friday\u2019s Game 3, even though their 18th-ranked bench had outplayed the No. 1 bench in the league. The Toronto Raptors were missing key reserve Fred VanVleet for all but three minutes of the first two games, but their bench still had the ability to withstand his absence if the other reserves had performed up to their standards. They didn\u2019t, but the Raptors had outscored the Wizards 134-95 in 46 minutes with Toronto\u2019s two All-Stars (Kyle Lowry and DeMar DeRozan) and Washington\u2019s two All-Stars (John Wall and Bradley Beal) all on the floor. And that was the difference. The Wizards, given their talent, are the most dangerous 8 seed the Eastern Conference has had since the last 1-8 upset (2012, when the No. 1 seed Chicago Bulls lost Derrick Rose in Game 1). But while their reserves, led by Mike Scott, stepped up in Toronto, their starters hadn\u2019t done their share. The Wizards, however, finally put it all together on Friday. And it started with Wall and Beal, who combined for 56 points on 22-for-42 shooting in a 122-103 victory to make sure that this series will be heading back to Toronto for a Game 5. Wall added 14 assists and the pair accounted for seven of the Wizards\u2019 10 steals as they slowed down the Toronto offense for the first time in the series. It looked like more of the same at the start, with the Toronto starters building a nine-point lead in the first quarter. But the Washington bench, along with Beal, got the lead back by the end of the period. Then Wall registered 13 points and six assists in a 39-point second quarter. Leading 69-61 at the break, Washington held what had been the postseason\u2019s most efficient offense to just 42 points over the final 24 minutes. The Wizards switched more often and played pick-and-rolls more conservatively in Game 3, allowing defenders not involved in the action to stay at home on Toronto\u2019s shooters and close passing lanes. DeRozan and Lowry were held somewhat in check and the Wizards outscored the Raptors by four points with all four All-Star guards on the floor. The Washington bench continued to play well, with Kelly Oubre Jr. providing some support to Mike Scott, who now has the best plus-minus (plus-33) in the series. The improvement of the bench didn\u2019t matter in the first two games. But it held up on Friday, in part because Beal scored as many points (28) as he did in the first two games combined. \u201cBrad came out and was looking to go towards the basket and not just letting them hold him and going along with it,\u201d Wizards coach Scott Brooks said afterward. \u201cHe didn\u2019t want to dance with his opponent, he wanted to get away from them. That was a critical part of his success.\u201d With Wall playing downhill most of the night, Game 3 was more about how good the Wizards can be than how disappointing the Wizards have been. \u201cIt starts with us,\u201d Beal said. \u201cYou see the results,\u201d Kelly Oubre Jr. added. \u201cWhen those guys are at the top of their game, they\u2019re unstoppable. So it\u2019s their job to lock in and take the next step of being great and bringing out that same fire every game.\u201d The \u201cevery game\u201d thing is the issue. The Wizards have been frustratingly inconsistent all season. Good wins have often followed by inexplicable losses. While the Raptors were Team TCB (takin\u2019 care of business) this season, going 35-2 against the 12 teams that finished with losing records, the Wizards were just 20-15 against that same group. On the other hand, the Wizards were 7-5 against the top three teams in the East and were one of two East teams (the Raptors were the other) to beat the Houston Rockets with both Chris Paul and James Harden in the lineup. The ceiling is high, but times when the Wizards reached it have been fleeting. On Friday, the Wizards played like the team they were projected to be when this season started and not the team that finished a disappointing eighth in the East. But nobody can know what\u2019s to come in Game 4 on Sunday (6 p.m. ET, TNT). \u201cWhen you\u2019re in the playoffs, you can\u2019t rest on the last game,\u201d Brooks said. \u201cIt\u2019s always about playing better the next game. We can\u2019t lose our edge.\u201d John Schuhmann is a staff writer for NBA.com. You can e-mail him here , find his archive here and follow him on Twitter . "},{"id":40819,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/unheralded-kentucky-shai-gilgeous-alexander-built-himself-promising-prospect","date":"2018-04-21","title":"Kentucky guard Shai Gilgeous-Alexander grew into becoming a leader and promising prospect during freshman season","content":" Moments after Kentucky won the Southeastern Conference tournament title in March, league commissioner Greg Sankey asked coach John Calipari who were his captains so he could present them the trophy. Trouble was, during a season of constant evolution for the youngest team he\u2019d ever coached, Calipari hadn\u2019t gotten around to picking captains. For the longest time, he wasn\u2019t quite sure who to trust with that responsibility. But one of the players he sent to collect the hardware was freshman guard Shai Gilgeous-Alexander, who by that time had become de facto captain. \u201cHe [became] that guy because of how he works, how he performs, how he finishes games,\u201d Calipari said the next week during the NCAA Tournament. \u201cPlayers are now looking to him. The best way to have captains is kind of [let them] evolve versus \u2018I\u2019m naming you a captain.\u2019 I\u2019d rather it be that way.\u201d Gilgeous-Alexander, a 6-foot-6 point guard from Canada, was the least heralded recruit in Kentucky\u2019s 2017 class. Of the seven players signed by Calipari and his staff, five were rated five-star prospects and Gilgeous-Alexander, a consensus four-start recruit, was ranked seventh among them. That he wound up having the biggest impact on the team was no surprise to people who know him best. Raised by demanding parents \u2014 his mother, Charmaine Gilgeous, was on Canada\u2019s Olympic track team and sends her son text message critiques of his play after every game \u2014 Gilgeous-Alexander developed mental toughness and the willingness to be coached. Calipari doesn\u2019t go easy on his players, but nothing he threw at the young guard did anything but strengthen his resolve to improve and take his place as the Wildcats\u2019 leader. Gilgeous-Alexander\u2019s journey to stardom at Kentucky began in his native Canada, but continued in Chattanooga, Tenn., where Zach Ferrell has built a prep powerhouse at Hamilton Heights Christian Academy. Wanting to test his ability against better players, Gilgeous-Alexander came to the U.S. in the summer of 2015. Unheralded at the time, it took just a couple of months for him to begin attracting interest from power conference schools. Florida\u2019s Mike White was the first to offer a scholarship. Asked what attracted him to Gilgeous-Alexander, White quickly offered a brief, but on point answer. \u201cHis length, hands, touch, steadiness, and communication skills,\u201d White said. Gilgeous-Alexander committed to the Gators, but White and his staff knew it would be another year before they could lock him up with signed scholarship papers. A lot could happen in that time, and it did. In July 2016, Gilgeous-Alexander earned a spot on Canada\u2019s U18 team that competed in the FIBA Americas Championship and won the silver medal. He averaged 7.8 points, but more important, a tournament-high 5.4 assists and 3.0 steals. By October, he decided to reopen his commitment. He\u2019d been a fan of Calipari\u2019s Kentucky teams, and reached out to Calipari before the latter could contact him. By Halloween, he received an offer from Kentucky. Two weeks later, he was a Wildcat.  NBA scouts quickly realized that among the talent Calipari had collected, Gilgeous-Alexander was different. Calipari told the press he had an \u201cold man\u2019s,\u201d game, meaning old school, in a good way. Oscar Robertson used to say that if his opponent gave him a 15-foot shot, he\u2019d look for a 10-footer, and if he had a 10-footer, he\u2019d seek a five-footer, and if he had that, well, why not go get a layup? That was Gilgeous-Alexander. There were other plusses, too. With his height and wingspan, Gilgeous-Alexander could guard multiple positions, and he was a willing defender. And when he started showing everyone he could score at a high level, he shot up draft boards. Gilgeous-Alexander\u2019s 24 points against Louisville \u2014 \u201cShai was ridiculous,\u201d Cal said \u2014 were an eye opener. And as the season progressed, more often than not, it was Gilgeous-Alexander \u2014 who averaged 14.4 points, 4.1 rebounds, a team-high 5.10 assists, a team-high 1.6 steals and shot 40.4 from 3 \u2014 who carried the Wildcats. They endured a four-game losing streak during the first two weeks of February, but after Calipari made a couple of adjustments, allowing his players to focus more on attacking the basket and scoring than micro-managing them on defense, Kentucky took off. The Wildcats rebounded with four straight wins, lost at Florida in their season finale, then won three games in three days to capture their fourth straight SEC Tournament title. Gilgeous-Alexander had his fingerprints all over the trophy well before Sankey handed it to him \u2014 in the title game against Tennessee he collected 29 points, seven boards, three assists and two steals. He was an easy pick for tournament MVP. Kentucky continued that momentum into the NCAA Tournament, though its run was cut short in the Sweet 16. Gilgeous-Alexander averaged 20.3 points. Asked during the NCAAs to explain how he took over such a talented team, SGA attributed it to accepting Calipari\u2019s coaching. \u201cAbout midpoint during the season, I watched a lot of film,\u201d he said. \u201cI was really taking what coach was asking of me, what this team needed me to do. I just tackled it. Once I cleared that role out, I worked on my game and got better every day.\u201d Though Kentucky fell short of its goal to win the national championship, Gilgeous-Alexander more than demonstrated he was worthy of a lottery pick. And a coach who worked with him longer than Calipari, Hamilton Heights\u2019 Ferrell, thinks he knows how Gilgeous-Alexander will be utilized at the next level. \u201cI think he\u2019s a point guard,\u201d Farrell said. \u201cThrough and through. I\u2019ve never known him to be anything but a point guard. He came to Kentucky and people were saying he\u2019s a combo. They must have watched some film or seen something that I didn\u2019t see the last couple of years.\u201d Farrell has his reasons for projecting Gilgeous-Alexander as a point. \u201cHe\u2019s got incredible feel off the ball screen, which is so much what the NBA is; he can really get to the basket,\u201d Farrell said. \u201cIf you saw Kentucky this year \u2014 if their kids were making outside shots, you weren\u2019t beating them. If they were making outside shots and you had to stay with them, that means Shai was getting to the basket every time. \u201c[In the NCAA Tournament] Buffalo stayed with Kentucky\u2019s shooters more and Shai lit them up [27 points on 10-of-12 shooting]. But Kansas State [which beat the Wildcats in the Sweet 16] said \u2018you\u2019re not getting to the basket, guys are going to have to make shots.\u2019 In the NBA, it\u2019s not going to be that. Shea\u2019s position at the point will give him some phenomenal opportunities to create for others or get to the rim.\u201d * * * Chris Dortch is the editor of the Blue Ribbon College Basketball Yearbook . The Celtics went on to sweep the Bulls in that series en route to winning the NBA title in 1986. \u201cI didn\u2019t think anyone was capable of doing what Michael has done to us,\u201d marveled Celtics star Larry Bird after Game 2. \u201cHe is the most exciting, awesome player in the game today. Sure, McCutchen\u2019s 25 years of experience as one of the league\u2019s highest-rated game officials \u2014 including 169 playoff contests and 16 Finals games \u2014 would inform his new position with a real, recent perspective, feeding his effectiveness in working with the full staff of refs. On the other hand, coming so soon after the retirement of veterans Joey Crawford and Danny Crawford, taking McCutchen off the court would thin the ranks of proven referees. McCutchen believes he can help enough in his new macro- job, though, to make up for any losses at the micro- level. The native of San Angelo, Texas, who is known for a fashion sense built around a wardrobe of colorful, throwback \u201czoot suits,\u201d spoke with NBA.com in a wide-ranging interview. This Q&A is edited from that longer conversation: NBA.com : How does the dress code in your new job compare to your old one? Monty McCutchen : I put it in the contract, verbally, don\u2019t be tellin\u2019 me to wear gray and navy. It\u2019s not happening. NBA.com : How about the job itself, five months in? MM : I\u2019m enjoying the job. It\u2019s a different pace to my life. One of the things that\u2019s great about the new job is, you don\u2019t realize the amount of support that exists for referees when you\u2019re sort of out in the desert going from game to game. It\u2019s easy to paint your own picture of victimization and think, \u2018Aw, nobody loves me.\u2019 I\u2019m finding out that\u2019s just not true. The people I\u2019m getting to work with now [at NBA HQ] are sort of life-giving people. They\u2019re really enjoyable people. That\u2019s exciting at 52 years old, to take on something new and lets you grow. NBA.com : So you\u2019re feeling the love now that you\u2019re management. You weren\u2019t feeling that when you were labor? MM : I\u2019m not making bad calls anymore [laughs]. NBA.com : The just-completed regular season was marked by a lot of talk about player\/referee friction. Where do you feel things stand? MM : You\u2019re never solving all the missed calls. The work is imperfect. What we can have an impact on is the culture of mutual respect and how we disagree, and the culture of good people doing good work \u2014 which is what our referees are \u2014 can be respected even amongst the disagreements. That\u2019s what I hope to have an impact on. NBA.com : Danny Crawford, who retired last year, told me early this season he thinks officiating accuracy is about as good as it\u2019s going to get \u2014 and will be at approximately the same level 10 years from now. How much improvement is humanly possible? MM : That begs a question of what you value. For me, if you only value product, then it\u2019s about a percentage [of correct calls], right? I know that to the people we impact \u2014 our players, our coaches, our owners \u2014 product is really important. And it\u2019s important to me too. But process is also important. Regardless of what the potential outcome is, it\u2019s an integrity-based decision to be involved in the process to try to get better. To put the work in to get better. If you view life through that process, then the product will take care of itself. NBA.com : Give us an example of one area to be addressed. MM : With the use of modern technology in its various forms, with our replay center , with our ability to get information out more quickly to our officials, I think all of that will speed up the learning curve of our lesser-experienced officials. Our staff has the opportunity to become more consistent through rapid education. It can compress the difference between Kenny Mauer in year 32 or 33, whatever year he\u2019s in, and Ray Acosta, J.B. DeRosa and Jonathan Sterling in year 1. Then, when you put the work in, you live with the results. \u2026 We won\u2019t get to 100 percent. In that way, Danny is 100 percent right. But I will say, what is 100 percent? If we watch a game, we can\u2019t even agree what 100 percent would be. NBA.com : There often seems to be a style vs. substance component in play. For instance, early in retired referee Steve Javie\u2019s career, players and coaches praised his ability to adjudicate a game but felt he was arrogant and tough to talk to. Skills in both areas are essential, aren\u2019t they? MM : For years, none of [the latter] has been taught. Referees have sort of figured that out on their own. What we\u2019re hopeful to do is to start to implement some programs. We have a sports psychologist who helped us with our WNBA preseason meetings who gave a really great foundation for how to approach these interpersonal [relations]. If we start to educate and evaluate and say, hey, this matters \u2014 so does accuracy, but this matters \u2014 it pushes people in the direction of finding answers for themselves. I don\u2019t want to get too touchy-feely here. We need to understand this is an important part of refereeing. It doesn\u2019t mean acquiescing all the time. It just means being able to articulate a position. NBA.com : I\u2019ve heard from former referees that some of the younger officials don\u2019t arrive with as many skills or as much life experience as in the past. MM : And what we\u2019ve found is that, often, no choice is a choice. If I don\u2019t know what to say in my younger years of being a referee, if I don\u2019t know how to meet the will of the coaches and the players, I tend to default to nothing. I say nothing, I hope this goes away and I avoid the situation. What we\u2019re finding out from traveling around to various teams is, that non-choice is leading to the idea of arrogance, of being ignored, which of course amps up emotions. It doesn\u2019t calm them down. NBA.com : This is an emotional game, with players and coaches competing with a high intensity. Referees are supposed to stay cool and detached, right? MM : The one thing I would throw out for open discourse is that poise is an important part of all of our lives. Playing a game is emotional. Having things screamed at you is emotional as well. The fact is, we\u2019ve practiced and trained to remain poised, for the most part. We all can grow from more poise. NBA.com : Let me ask about some specific issues related to officiating. Like the idea of \u201cmake-up calls.\u201d In Minnesota\u2019s Game 1 loss to Houston , Jeff Teague inadvertently got hit in the jaw but the refs seemed to not see it. Moments later, with the Timberwolves\u2019 bench in uproar, Houston lost possession on a traveling call. And the color analyst working the game immediately labeled that a \u201cmake-up call.\u201d MM : I think it\u2019s the lowest hanging fruit. It\u2019s lazy reporting. And to be honest, it means someone doesn\u2019t have to put any real thought into what just happened. The fact is, we\u2019re scrutinized to such a degree that if you were to think that way, all you would be is wrong twice. And wrong twice gets you sent home earlier. What we\u2019re graded on is a percentage basis of correct and non-correct calls. We have consequences to our decisions. Was there actually a travel? OK, we missed the first call \u2014 I understand that. But do you know how many times I know I missed that call? Very few. It\u2019s only later that I understand I missed the call. This also might come as some surprise to you \u2014 I\u2019m being facetious and hopefully funny \u2014 that players will tell you things sometimes that aren\u2019t true. And only the tape will tell you that later on. If you go around making up calls, only to find out you were correct in the original case, look how far down the rabbit hole you would have gone. NBA.com : OK, how about \u201cstar treatment?\u201d In the same category? MM : Absolutely. Every one of our teams thinks that every superstar gets every call \u2014 except theirs. That\u2019s the nature of the business. I understand that, I don\u2019t begrudge anyone that sense of protecting their player. But you know what, the game is moving really, really fast. And the only way our fans can believe that the championship their team won or fell just short of is legitimate is if they believe in the integrity of our officials. Our officials want to get plays right whether it\u2019s the best players or players who are on 10-day contracts. Anyone who doesn\u2019t \u2014 and I come to know about it \u2014 won\u2019t be here for long. NBA.com : Why doesn\u2019t the NBA keep referees together in a \u201ccrew\u201d through a playoff series the way baseball does with its umpires? MM : I think you would build up a frustration level. With baseball, you get a different dynamic in a home-plate umpire every game in a series. That is not our work. If you were to have a [controversial] play at the end of Game 1, that crew would have to be living with the commentary and both teams would have to live with the thought that things weren\u2019t right. I think it\u2019s important that every game starts with a fresh slate. NBA.com : It\u2019s as if your refs all call balls and strikes every night. MM : That\u2019s right. NBA.com : Another playoff topic is how the points for flagrant fouls and technicals accumulate through the playoffs. That turned into a big deal for Golden State and Draymond Green two years ago. Is that an area where the referees have input or is it strictly up to the Competition Committee? MM : We have a non-voting voice in the Competition Committee \u2014 two of our current referees and myself are on that as part of the meetings, but without a vote. We can give our perspective but it\u2019s an administrative decision that\u2019s above my pay grade. But for me, I\u2019ll revert back philosophically to my point that poise is an important part of a championship pursuit. NBA.com : The officials working the playoffs continue to be evaluated and the initial group of 36 eventually narrows to 12 for The Finals. Someone always is watching? MM : Your body of work is evaluated. Just like missing one layup, we\u2019re not going to punish someone\u2019s career over one mistake. But if consistent work is not being produced, that hinders your ability to move on to serve the game. The game must be served at this time of year to its highest level. NBA.com : Second only perhaps to air traffic controllers, referees are reviewed and scrutinized more than almost any other profession, it seems. Can they ever just relax? MM : You have to own your ability to relax. We all took this job on, we all knew it\u2019s a highly scrutinized profession. Those of us who accept the work and the responsibility for it don\u2019t have any trouble getting on a treadmill the next day and working off a little stress. That doesn\u2019t mean you shake your mistakes as if they don\u2019t matter. Mistakes, when they impact game, stick with the referee. But they can\u2019t stick so much that they affect the next game\u2019s work. The participants in that game deserve our best work, not some victimized referee who\u2019s moping around because he or she feels too much pressure. NBA.com : Before and since the All-Star break \u2014 where a small group of players and referees gathered for a summit meeting \u2014 you and others from the league have met with all 30 teams to address officiating concerns. How has that gone? MM : We often want to put situations in opposition to one another, when in fact they\u2019re capable of moving along the same path that leads to a beneficial place. Our two unions meeting can\u2019t possibly be anything but a good thing. It\u2019s people talking about how to solve a problem. People looking inward about how to participate in that. That\u2019s great. Michelle Johnson (senior VP and head of referee operations), Shareef Abdur-Rahim (associate VP of basketball operations) and myself visited the teams and laid down a template for what good, honest discourse and constructive criticism can be about. We were open to listening to teams\u2019 perspective \u2026 and we were able to give a perspective and education about how hard our group [works], how much tape they watch and preparation they put in. In a profession that\u2019s been so good to me, to help other people have success and to see the league have success, I find that to have a great deal of meaning. * * * Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. The statistics are through the 2017 Playoffs. \u2022 Teams that win the first game of a best-of-seven series go on to win the series 76.6% of the time (393-120). \u2022 Teams that win the first game of a best-of-seven series at home go on to win the series 85.4% of the time (321-55). \u2022 Teams that win the first game of a best-of-seven series on the road go on to win the series 52.6% of the time (72-65). \u2022 Teams that win the first two games of a best-of-seven series go on to win the series 93.5% of the time (273-19). \u2022 Teams that win the first two games of a best-of-seven series at home go on to win the series 94.0% of the time (236-15). \u2022 Teams that win the first two games of a best-of-seven series on the road go on to win the series 84.0% of the time (21-4). \u2022 Teams that win Game 3 of a 1-1 best-of-seven series go on to win the series 73.3% of the time (162-59). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 2-1 go on to win the series 79.8% of the time (308-78). \u2022 Teams that win the first three games of a best-of-seven series go on to win the series 100% of the time (127-0). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 3-1 go on to win the series 95.4% of the time (226-11). \u2022 Teams that win Game 5 of a 2-2 best-of-seven series go on to win the series 82.8% of the time (164-34). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 3-2 go on to win the series 84.8% of the time (251-45). History of best-of-seven series in NBA playoffs (since 1984) Below are statistics about the history of best-of-seven series since the 16-team playoff format began in 1984. The statistics are through the 2017 Playoffs. \u2022 Teams that win the first game of a best-of-seven series go on to win the series 76.8% of the time (275-83). \u2022 Teams that win the first game of a best-of-seven series at home go on to win the series 86.0% of the time (228-37) \u2022 Teams that win the first game of a best-of-seven series on the road go on to win the series 50.5% of the time (47-46). \u2022 Teams that win the first two games of a best-of-seven series go on to win the series 93.1% of the time (201-15). \u2022 Teams that win the first two games of a best-of-seven series at home go on to win the series 93.9% of the time (184-12). \u2022 Teams that win the first two games of a best-of-seven series on the road go on to win the series 85.0% of the time (17-3). \u2022 Teams that win Game 3 of a 1-1 best-of-seven series go on to win the series 71.8% of the time (102-40). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 2-1 go on to win the series 79.2% of the time (210-55). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 3-0 have gone on to win the series 100% of the time (93-0). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 3-1 go on to win the series 95.6% of the time (152-7). \u2022 Teams that win Game 5 of a 2-2 best-of-seven series go on to win the series 84.1% of the time (116-22). \u2022 Teams that lead a best-of-seven series 3-2 go on to win the series 85.6% of the time (172-29). * * *"},{"id":46952,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/philadelphia-76ers-joel-embiid-status-game-3-miami-heat","date":"2018-04-19","title":"Philadelphia 76ers' Joel Embiid upgraded to start for Game 3","content":" Philadelphia 76ers All-Star center Joel Embiid made it clear recently he wants to play in the postseason . "},{"id":40838,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/houston-rockets-dig-defense-then-rout-minnesota-timberwolves-game-2","date":"2018-04-19","title":"Houston Rockets dig in on defense, then catch fire in Game 2 rout","content":"HOUSTON \u2014 The deeper Mike D\u2019Antoni digs into his bag of tricks against the Minnesota Timberwolves, the more interesting the Houston Rockets become. He\u2019s got an answer for any and everything you throw at him in this first-round playoff series against the Minnesota Timberwolves, and the proof was found in Houston\u2019s 102-82 win in Game 2 . The Rockets are not some one-trick outfit that lives or dies by the 3-point line. Even with the superstar point guard duo of James Harden and Chris Paul leading the way, they are much more complete than that. This is a group just as capable of locking down on the defensive end as they are of overwhelming the opposition with one of the vintage 3-point showers D\u2019Antoni\u2019s team have been famous for over the years. That defensive component \u2014 the result of assistant coach Jeff Bzdelik and Houston\u2019s role players \u2014 was on full display in Wednesday night\u2019s romp against Minnesota. Even with a much-improved defensive presence, it\u2019s always nice to lean on an old standby if the situation demands it \u2014 the way it did in the opening minutes of Game 2 when D\u2019Antoni went searching for the right buttons to push with his starters struggling. He went to Gerald Green and he kicked off the fireworks, draining four of his seven shots from deep during an electric six-minute stretch of the second quarter that saw the Rockets flip a seven-point deficit into a 16-point lead, a cushion that ballooned to 27 before it was all over. \u201cSooner or later,\u201d D\u2019Antoni said, \u201cwe\u2019ve got too many guys, somebody\u2019s going to catch fire, you hope. Gerald did, he came in when we were struggling and hit a couple of threes and opened it up. Then once you see the ball go in, once you get the monkey off your back and all that stuff and then we played well for about two and a half quarters. Defensively, we played well the whole game and that\u2019s what it takes.\u201d Green\u2019s story is just as wild as his style \u2014 from his braids to the throwback Warren Moon Houston Oilers jersey he wore to the podium after the game as he sat next to Harden. A Houston native, Green was waived by the Milwaukee Bucks on the opening day of the season and was working out in his hometown when the Rockets called. They needed another perimeter player after Christmas, when James Harden was lost for seven games in January to a Grade 2 hamstring strain. Green was signed three days after Christmas and played the same night in Boston, going scoreless in his 11-minute season debut. But he proved to be an indispensable component on what was arguably the deepest and most balanced roster in the league. Now he epitomizes the depth, seasoning and talent on what is perhaps the most peculiarly effective playoff roster. \u201cTo me this is more than a dream,\u201d Green said after recording playoff career-highs in points (21) and rebounds (12). This moment right here that I\u2019m living right now is everything I dreamed about since I was five or six years old. Right now, I\u2019m staying in the moment but I\u2019m staying locked in because I still have a bigger goal we\u2019re trying to achieve. It\u2019s not just about getting to the playoffs. It\u2019s about getting a championship and bringing a championship to Houston. We\u2019ve still got a long way to go but I feel like I\u2019m as focused as I\u2019ve ever been on my dream.\u201d Harden and Paul share the same dream, only with the extra burden of being the faces of this Rockets team. They\u2019ve also shared tag-team duties in the rough outing department after the first two games of this series. Paul struggled in the opener (14 points, four assists, six turnovers), but bounced back Wednesday night with a classic line (27 points, eight assists, one turnover). Meanwhile, Harden shot a miserable 2-for-18 (and 1-for-10 on 3-pointers) in Game 2, finishing with 12 points after his 44-point, eight-assist night in Game 1. But there\u2019s so much more at work here, explained Harden, that he doesn\u2019t stress over off nights like he once did. \u201cI\u2019ve got better players around me,\u201d Harden said. \u201cBetter players on both ends of the floor that have my back and I know that. So yeah, I don\u2019t worry about me shooting the basketball. I can miss. I missed what, 16 shots today. I\u2019m going to shoot the ball, when you you\u2019ve got guys behind me telling me to \u2018keep shooting, that\u2019s your shot.\u2019 I\u2019m going to keep shooting. Then on the defensive end I just have to make sure I\u2019m locked in and create the energy, make sure my rotations are really good and keep communicating.\u201d Communicating on a roster that seems to fit together perfectly, with the contributions coming from all directions. Young center Clint Capela is an emerging talent and Houston is 44-3 with Harden, Paul and Capela in the lineup. Veteran forward Trevor Ariza, who has played for six different teams in his 14 seasons, has championship experience (Los Angeles Lakers, 2009) and can work on both ends. Rugged-yet-undersized power forward P.J. Tucker lacks that championship ring, but has defensive chops, too. Eric Gordon is the reigning Kia Sixth Man of the Year. And D\u2019Antoni can milk quality minutes out of the likes of a physical big man (16-year veteran Nene) and a steady guard (former All-Star Joe Johnson), if needed. And that doesn\u2019t account for two of D\u2019Antoni\u2019s regulars \u2014 forwards Ryan Anderson (left ankle sprain) and Luc Mbah a Moute (dislocated right shoulder) \u2014 who were in street clothes tonight. \u201cThey are a good team,\u201d Timberwolves forward Andrew Wiggins said. \u201cTheir team doesn\u2019t consist of one guy. They have a lot of good players that can put the ball in the hoop and lot of people that can create, so I think we did a good job on [Harden]. Now we have to stop the others.\u201d That might not be nearly as easy as it sounds. * * * Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. Based purely on that, the possibilities along with the scales seemed tilted in their favor Wednesday. And when the buzzer sounded and the outcome was decided and fans rubbed their disbelieving eyes, those Oklahoma City Thunder\u2019s star trio found themselves on the bad side of the 102-95 score . Not only did they lose, but they were collectively outscored 13-2 in the fourth by Jazz rookie Donovan Mitchell \u2026 who just wrapped up his second career playoff game. This Western Conference first-round series is tied 1-1 because the Jazz revealed themselves for who they are, and in a big way. They\u2019re a dogged defensive team, perhaps the league\u2019s best, and they\u2019re led almost exclusively by a kid (in NBA years anyway) \u2014 although Mitchell never really looked like a rookie from the time he first suited up. He\u2019s playing a beefy role, the kind that rookies don\u2019t normally do or get because they lack the ability, wisdom and quite simply aren\u2019t put in that position by their coach. Those rules don\u2019t apply to Mitchell, especially when the game gets tense. His number is called more than a hotline and that hasn\u2019t changed even here, in the postseason and when matched against the reigning Kia MVP in Westbrook. \u201cWe\u2019ve given him that respect all season,\u201d said Joe Ingles, \u201cand there\u2019s no reason to take it from him now.\u201d Mitchell led the Jazz in scoring this season with 20.5 points per game and played 33.4 minutes per game. Only a handful of rookies in NBA history have led a playoff team in scoring, most of those are in the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame. Utah made him the team\u2019s lead singer almost instantly, partly because he proved himself right away, but also by necessity. After losing Gordon Hayward to free agency, nobody on the club brought the goods to wear that big a backpack. And when Rodney Hood was dealt at midseason, the Jazz lost their primary No. 2 option as well. None of it mattered to Donovan or affected his flow. He won the Verizon Slam Dunk contest during All-Star weekend yet also shot a respectable 34 percent on 3-pointers. He\u2019ll be in the running for Kia Rookie of the Year , and while Ben Simmons of the Philadelphia 76ers will probably win that award, Mitchell has pole position for Rookie of the Playoffs \u2014 especially after Wednesday. Rookies tend to freeze in these situations where the intensity doubles. For Mitchell, it was motivation. Everyone\u2019s engaged and giving you information, telling you what needs to be done, and what needs to be changed. But there were other things going on for the Cavaliers as they beat the Pacers to even the best-of-seven series at 1-1 , and Lue did have a hand in them. Specifically, Lue reconfigured his starting lineup, swapping in J.R. Smith and Kyle Korver for Jeff Green and Rodney Hood. And the moves panned out, with both veteran shooting guards contributing at Quicken Loans Arena. Korver scored 12 points and played his underrated team-defense role, taking a pair of charges and diving to the floor in ways that might break most 37-year-olds. Smith, for so long in his career an unrepentant chucker, got by with only five shots but accepted the defensive challenge of guarding Indiana\u2019s primary scorer, Victor Oladipo. Smith got a pair of steals, including a pivotal one with three minutes left when he tied up Oladipo in the backcourt and turned it into a layup for a 93-86 lead. It was the 16th of the Pacers\u2019 17 turnovers, a lethal number on a night that began so brutally. Let\u2019s put it this way: James, for all the havoc he wreaked early, finished a plus-7 in plus\/minus. Smith was a plus-8. And Korver was a plus-15. \u201cEvery moment is big in the playoffs,\u201d Korver said afterward. \u201cMoments can change games, moments can create a run. So I think that\u2019s on all our minds. If there\u2019s a loose ball, dive on the floor. If you can take a charge, do that. The playoffs are all about scratching. The same goes for the playoff debut of Minnesota Timberwolves All-Star center Karl-Anthony Towns. Timberwolves coach Tom Thibodeau had one view in the immediate aftermath of the Timberwolves\u2019 Game 1 loss to the Houston Rockets Sunday night, when Towns struggled in his playoff debut, scoring just eight points on 3-for-9 shooting, well below his season average of 21.3 points. Thibodeau said his big man had to be more active to deal with he swarming and double-teaming defense the Rockets threw at him. But that uncharacteristic performance \u2014 Towns was held to fewer than eight points just twice in the regular season \u2014 looked much different after a thorough study of the film. \u201cAfter watching the film I thought he made a lot of good plays,\u201d Thibodeau said after the Timberwolves wrapped up practice Tuesday at Rice University. \u201cWe\u2019ve had a hard time guarding them in the regular season and there\u2019s still a lot of things we have to do better. But offensively, I thought he made good plays. I think he understands what he has to do, he\u2019ll be fine.\u201d The Timberwolves need Towns to be better than just \u201cfine\u201d in Game 2 Wednesday ( 8:30 p.m. ET, TNT ), particularly if his Rockets counterpart, Clint Capela (24 points, 12 rebounds and three blocks in Game 1) continues to play at a high level in this series. Towns clearly didn\u2019t take offense to Thibodeau\u2019s initial review of his performance, agreeing with his coach after Game 1 that he\u2019s \u201cgot to be better on both sides of the basketball.\u201d But he reiterated several times Tuesday that his objective is to stick with the game plan rather than getting caught up in any game inside the game, just to satisfy someone else\u2019s expectations of what a player of his considerable talents should be able to do in this setting. He did, however, acknowledge the strategic differences between the regular season and postseason atmosphere, and he\u2019ll be sure to adjust accordingly. \u201cI felt good out there, and I never take anything too high or too low. I always stay even-keeled,\u201d he said. \u201cIt felt to me like a regular game, but it\u2019s more about playing chess this time, you know. It\u2019s not about playing checkers, it\u2019s about playing chess. And it\u2019s a game about who is the more disciplined team, who sticks to their game plan the most and finds ways to adjust on the fly.\u201d Those adjustments for Towns must include taking advantage of the Rockets\u2019 endless switching on defense, situations where he ends up with a much smaller perimeter player matched up on him. He\u2019ll need to be more aggressive offensively, even if it requires a slight tweak to the game plan he mentioned repeatedly Tuesday.  \u201cYou\u2019ve got to do it when the game seems that it can be taken, or when its getting out of hand,\u201d Town said. \u201cI have to do a better job of assessing the situations and adjusting on the fly quicker, especially in the playoffs.\u201d It helps that the Timberwolves were within striking distance at the end on a night when they didn\u2019t get the best out of Towns. The same way the Rockets don\u2019t expect to struggle through another 10-for-37 shooting performance from beyond the 3-point line or for their superstar point guard Chris Paul to turn the ball over the way he did, the Timberwolves don\u2019t foresee another subpar performance from one of the league\u2019s elite big men. \u201cJust makes us more hungry, more confident,\u201d Towns said. \u201cWe felt we played well, but we made some mistakes here and there and it ended up costing us in the end. But if we can tweak things here and there, I think we can be something really special and we could possibly win the series.\u201d As for any suggestions that he needs to be more of a priority, as suggested by TNT analyst Charles Barkley after the game, Towns and his teammates were careful to sidestep any outside influence on the situation. Jimmy Butler issued a quick, \u201cnope,\u201d when asked for a response and ended his media obligation immediately after that. Towns took a more nuanced approach. \u201cI mean, we almost won the game,\u201d he said. \u201cSo you\u2019ve got to take it as its given. You\u2019ve got to go with the flow of the game. It\u2019s about following the game plan. We went with the flow of the game, had ourselves a great chance to win at the end. We were up with six minutes left, so our game plans were working. We cost ourselves as players, not as coaches, late in the game not getting some stops and James (Harden) making some tough shots. You can\u2019t control that. What transpired next was done behind closed doors and so we can only assume that Stephen Curry did what Curry usually does in these situations: Dribble drills, shooting drills, rinse and repeat. Several hours later, though, at Oracle Arena, the only bounce he showed was when he rose from his chair during timeouts, wearing a plaid jacket and a satisfying grin. For the second time the Warriors didn\u2019t need Curry against the San Antonio Spurs which means everything is going according to plan for Golden State in this first round series. He can relax; the Warriors, so far anyway, got this. While Curry continues to mend from a knee injury, the Warriors continue to make a convincing case that they don\u2019t need him, at least not now, maybe not ever in the first round or even the second. Coach Steve Kerr made an interesting observation weeks ago after Curry went down, that they were good enough to beat anyone without him. So far, so \u2026 prophetic: It\u2019s 2-0 Warriors now in the best-of-seven after Monday\u2019s 116-101 victory , with no meaningful slippage in sight for the defending champions. To prove his point, Kerr put Andre Iguodala in Curry\u2019s starting point guard spot to start the series. It was a bold move but also an unintentionally arrogant one as well, because Iguodala has only started eight regular-season games in four years and has never played the position before in his 13-year NBA career. Iguodala opened Game 2 with three shots from deep, then settled in and supplied defense, some boards, a few chest passes and other intangibles that the Warriors have come to expect from him. He finished with 14 points, seven rebounds and five assists in just 27 minutes. This is what the Spurs are up against. \u201cI just love his poise,\u201d said Durant. \u201cHe\u2019s a veteran leader for us. A guy that just kind of keeps it real with everybody \u2026 he doesn\u2019t have an ego and he\u2019s going to go out there and do the best job he can do. Even if he\u2019s not making shots, he adds so much value to our team with his energy, his deflections, his rebounds, driving to the rim and getting hockey assists. We appreciate him for it.\u201d The Warriors were sloppy initially and trailed the Spurs by six at halftime after taking San Antonio\u2019s best shot, yet won by 15. That\u2019s because even without Curry, the reinforcements are deep, fully functional and clinically capable. The Spurs still have no resistance to offer for Durant and Klay Thompson, who combined for 63 points. And the Golden State defense once again held the Spurs under 42 percent shooting, an especially deadly number when LaMarcus Aldridge (34 points) is the only player worth a double team. \u201cNo one can make up Steph\u2019s contributions individually,\u201d said Thompson. \u201cThat\u2019s got to be done as a team, and even then it\u2019s hard, the way he can shoot the ball.\u201d Yes, a two-time Kia MVP isn\u2019t easily cloned. After Iguodala won Finals MVP honors in 2015, coincidentally by being elevated into the starting lineup in Game 4 of that series, he has become almost strictly a made-for-the-playoffs player. His numbers across the board have steadily dipped in the regular season, and the last two years he averaged 7.3 points and four rebounds. Last summer, Iguodala was a free agent and the Warriors were faced with perhaps their riskiest if not biggest financial decision of this championship era. Should they give considerable money to a player at age 34, especially with the payroll already soaring well into luxury tax hell? Durant did take less than his market value to give the Warriors some wiggle room, yet Iguodala demanded a three-year deal, and in almost any other situation, that many years tied up in an aging player would appear poisonous to a team. Indeed, Iguodala had feelers from other teams, namely the Houston Rockets \u2014 Golden State\u2019s biggest threat in the West. And so the Warriors caved, realizing that the possibility of Iguodala suiting up and possibly facing his old teammates in a key playoff series wouldn\u2019t be ideal. Asked if that would feel weird, he said, smiling: \u201cWell, I do know how to play these guys and I see them all the time in practice. But yeah, that would be strange.\u201d Boasting a roster rich in talent with four All-Stars, the Warriors can afford to cover for Iguodala in the regular season as long as he breaks from that slumber when the playoffs begin. They gave him that third year and his $48 million for what he can do for them from April through June, not September through March. \u201cYou try to be at your best around this time,\u201d he said. \u201cSo you take care of yourself, pace yourself and be ready.\u201d With Kawhi Leonard missing from the Spurs (and perhaps for good in the series ), the red alert for Iguodala might not flash until the next round or whenever the Warriors feel more threatened than they are now. Perhaps the West finals and against James Harden and the Rockets? That\u2019s the most likely scenario. That\u2019s where this appears to be heading. Until then, Kerr will keep Iguodala in the starting lineup against the Spurs and get, the Warriors hope, much of what he supplied in the first two games. \u201cThe biggest thing is not being afraid to fail,\u201d Iguodala said. \u201cYou try to embrace whatever situation you\u2019re put in. That\u2019s what I try to do. Being a starter, well, it\u2019s good to switch up at times. Sometimes you get complacent in that other role. Whatever they need from me, really, is fine.\u201d And that role is \u2026 point guard? Actually, Iguodala\u2019s strange promotion has more to do with his defensive mindset than his playmaking. Anyway, it\u2019s working and the Warriors are winning and they\u2019re doing it without one of the greatest shooters of all time. \u201cWe\u2019re just doing what we can until he gets back,\u201d said Thompson. \u201cHe\u2019s a special player and a big part of what we do, that\u2019s obvious.\u201d The clock on Curry\u2019s rehabilitation is another week or two, which would coincide with the start of the second round. Well, at this rate, by Sunday night, Curry might be joined by his teammates, who could finish off the Spurs and buy themselves an extended rest. This series isn\u2019t over, although it looks and feels like it is. This working out well for the Warriors. Wade\u2019s performance in Game 2 of the Miami Heat\u2019s first round series against the Philadelphia 76ers, 28 points on 11-for-16 shooting, was the difference in a 113-103 victory that gave the Heat home-court advantage as the series moves to Miami for Game 3 on Thursday. Wade is only here because the Cleveland Cavaliers decided they no longer needed him in their quest for a fourth straight trip to The Finals. After the Cavs made two major trades on deadline day in February, they decided that Wade wasn\u2019t needed, and they gave him a chance to return to the place he spent the first 13 years of his career. Wade got a hero\u2019s welcome upon his return to Miami, but he didn\u2019t give the Heat much of a boost. If anything, he compounded their offensive issues. He shot 41 percent in his 21 regular-season games with the Heat, and for the season as a whole, he had an effective field goal percentage of just 37 percent on shots from outside the paint, the second-worst mark among 207 players who attempted at least 200 shots from the outside. His lack of shooting, along with an inability to get to the basket like he used to, hurt the Miami offense. From the time the Heat reacquired him, they were almost 11 points per 100 possessions better offensively with Wade off the floor (scoring 111 per 100) than they were with him on the floor (scoring 100). Their defensive numbers were worse with him on the floor as well. On another team with another coach, another player would be out of the rotation with numbers like that. But this is Dwyane Wade on the Miami Heat, coached by Erik Spoelstra. The history with this player, this franchise and this coach can\u2019t be disregarded. \u201cYou put him in a Miami Heat uniform,\u201d Spoelstra said Monday, \u201cI don\u2019t care what his numbers were anywhere else or all year long or at different times. He came back late in the second to score the Heat\u2019s final six points of the half and build that lead to 14. Wade got into the paint off the dribble, pulled up from mid-range when the Sixers gave him space and took advantage of smaller guards in the post. There\u2019s a big difference between 2-0 and 1-1, and in the intensity of a back-and-forth affair, Wade seemed to be playing at his own pace. \u201cThere was a rhythm and a calmness to his game like he didn\u2019t feel too much,\u201d Brown said. \u201cAnd there was a real confidence the way he played to make those shots. \u201cThat is classic Dwyane Wade.\u201d A great individual performance made the team better. The Sixers said Greer died Saturday night in Arizona after a brief illness. He was 81. * Sixers release statement on passing of Hal Greer Greer spent 15 seasons with the Syracuse Nationals and Philadelphia 76ers and finished his career with a record 21,586 points. He\u2019s also the 76ers\u2019 career leader in field goals, field goals attempted, games and minutes played. Greer was the first player to have his number retired (15) by the 76ers in 1976. Greer also became the first player to be honored with a sculpture on 76ers Legends Walk at the team training complex in 2017. Greer made 10 straight All-Star games and earned All-Star Game MVP honors in 1968. He was also the second-leading scorer on Philadelphia\u2019s NBA championship team of 1966-67 and would earn a spot on the NBA 50th Anniversary All-Time Team in 1996. He played at Marshall and was the 13th overall pick by the Nationals in the 1958 draft. The Sixers will honor Greer before their playoff game against the Miami Heat on Monday night ( 8 ET, TNT ). The Sixers said Greer was survived by his wife Mayme, a son and two daughters."},{"id":40859,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-qa-lamarcus-aldridge-san-antonio-spurs","date":"2018-04-16","title":"Morning Tip Q&A: LaMarcus Aldridge","content":" If you had picked, say, a Martian off the planet Remulak at this time last year, sat him in front of a TV, and shown him highlights of the last two seasons of San Antonio Spurs basketball, then asked him which player on the team was the team\u2019s future, the Martian no doubt would have said something lik e \u201cklak nurdendi qliqui , \u201d which translates to \u201cthe man in the braids.\u201d And if you\u2019d then given him links to stories about the Spurs and asked which player was certain not to be here a year from now, the Martian would have no doubt said \u201cglaark vliblin motlovrok,\u201d which translates to \u201cLaMarcus Aldridge.\u201d Funny how Martians think. But they wouldn\u2019t have been alone; no one believed Aldridge had much of a shelf life in San Antonio. Rumors were plentiful before last June\u2019s Draft that the Spurs were shopping him , and they turned out to be true. Spurs coach Gregg Popovich acknowledged that, for the first time, a star player of his had asked to be dealt . Even more shockingly, Popovich copped that it was his fault, not Aldridge\u2019s, that things hadn\u2019t worked out since Aldridge had signed with the Spurs in 2014 , when he was among the biggest free-agent prizes available. Popovich vowed to put Aldridge back where he was comfortable, and followed through on it \u2014 not that he had many options, with Leonard missing almost all of the regular season and almost certainly all of the playoffs rehabbing a quad injury . With his back to the basket again, operating on the left block where he\u2019s operated much of his 12 NBA seasons and where he\u2019d played in Portland off Damian Lillard before coming to San Antonio, Aldridge again was an All-Star, putting up some of the most efficient numbers of his career ( 23.1 points and 8.5 rebounds per game ) and carrying the Spurs to the playoffs for the 21st straight season. \u201c Considering the way he\u2019s been playing and our recent success in those games, we are at the point where if LaMarcus gets a cough, a cold, we are in deep trouble,\u201d Manu Ginobili said after Aldridge left a late regular season game in Washington with a knee contusion. The Spurs remain a longshot themselves in the first round of the playoffs, but Aldridge, who got a three-year extension for $72 million at the start of the season, would now seem to be the guy with a long-term future in San Antonio. No translation necessary. Me: How have you all sustained this season through all the injuries and Kawhi not being here? LaMarcus Aldridge: Just having the next-man-up kind of mentality, having the system where you can plug in guys and they can understand, or they can help the team in the ways they\u2019re going to be good out there. And not giving in. It would have been easy to say we\u2019ve had all this adversity this year and it\u2019s not our year, but we have a bunch of guys that want to still be here. And we pushed through and guys got better, and the younger guys got some experience, and they\u2019ve gotten better. It\u2019s the whole team coming together. Me: What did you do to work with Dejounte [Murray] once Pop made it clear he was the guy at point guard ? LA: Just trying to talk to him. That big story came out about Dame and myself, and how we didn\u2019t talk . So I\u2019m trying to re-do that with him. He\u2019s not the same level of player offensively (as Lillard), but I\u2019m trying to talk with him more, mentor more as far as showing him things I\u2019ve learned over the years and showing him things he can do to make us better. And he\u2019s been great about listening, and he wants to get better. He\u2019s always in the gym. I feel like he\u2019s playing the toughest position out there, so it\u2019s going to take time. But he\u2019s definitely grown this year. Me: As a leader on this team, do you think you have to speak more? LA: I mean, that\u2019s definitely my role. That\u2019s who I\u2019ve grown to be. In the past, maybe not my first two years (in San Antonio), having Tim (Duncan), he\u2019s one of the best leaders in the game. I feel like this year, I\u2019ve definitely grown back to who I was. I\u2019ve been more vocal and tried to be that guy. I think guys have liked it. I\u2019ve had guys tell me at times I\u2019m not talking tonight. I think they\u2019ve gotten used to it. I\u2019ve definitely tried to stay on that and keep talking. Me: But at the other end of that, you have Tony [Parker] and Manu [Ginobili]. Do you defer to them? LA: They\u2019re both reserved guys. They talk at times. I think Manu talks more than Tony at times. They\u2019re both not really outspoken. So I try to take that burden of being the guy who\u2019s talking. They both speak when things have to be said. Me: Your numbers since the All-Star break are a little higher in terms of usage (31.2 after the break; 28.6 before), but your shots are different \u2014 fewer mid-range and more in the paint. Was that by design? LA: I mean, it\u2019s just what teams are giving you right now. I\u2019m a mid-range guy and most teams give that up, but that\u2019s what I do. So teams are not giving me that shot, so we do a lot of high-low stuff where I\u2019m at the rim or I\u2019m running the floor and trying to get something easy. And if I\u2019m out at the three and I\u2019m open, I shoot it, but that\u2019s not really what I want to do. For this team to be good I have to be mid-range and in, and let the other guys shoot threes. Me: Who is good for you in the high-low game? I know Pau [Gasol] has always been really good at it. LA: Pau\u2019s a great passer, a really skilled big. I think with him I\u2019m definitely better out there, because teams play me high or trap or whatever, and he\u2019s really good at putting that pass over the top. He\u2019s always looking for me. I think as any other big out there, he\u2019s the one that\u2019s always looking for me. Me: How rewarding has this season been for you? LA: It just feels good to be myself again. It wasn\u2019t really about trying to re-invent the wheel or do something I haven\u2019t done. This is who I\u2019ve been. So it felt good to be myself again and play my game again and get back to the level that I was at. It wasn\u2019t a surprise or anything new. This is just what I\u2019ve done. Me: But were you surprised when Pop said \u2018my fault?\u2019 LA: Yeah, for sure. As I\u2019ve told people, to have a Hall of Fame coach tell you that it was his fault, that he shouldn\u2019t have tried to change me, that was surreal to me. \u2018Cause I thought he was going to tell me \u2018go F yourself; I\u2019m Pop, you\u2019re wrong, I\u2019m right.\u2019 That\u2019s what you expect from someone of his caliber. It was the total opposite. He was like, you\u2019re right. I did try to change you and make you into my vision. You\u2019ve been in the league long enough; you\u2019ve done this. So I\u2019m going to leave you alone. Me: Do you have any expectation you\u2019re going to see Kawhi? LA: No, we\u2019re just playing. If you go day to day wondering when he\u2019s going to be back, then you\u2019re not in the moment and you\u2019re not going to do your job. You have to do your job and put your best foot forward and focus on what you have to do to win. If he comes back, great. But this is us right now, that\u2019s the attitude we have to have. Me: Any idea why it\u2019s been so hard for you to win on the road this year? LA: We shoot better at home as a group, so that\u2019s a part of it. I don\u2019t know. I just feel like we have to lock in. I feel (when) guys are at home they have the crowd behind them, and it\u2019s just better ju-ju and mojo, whatever it is. It\u2019s always better at home. But we have to figure it out, because in order to win it, you have to win on the road. We have guys that are definitely good enough to play home and away. We\u2019ve just got to lock in. https:\/\/twitter.com\/Jabari_Bird\/status\/984532746105163781%20 \u2014 Celtics rookie Jabari Bird (@Jabari_Bird), Thursday, 4:44 p.m., supposedly on FaceTime with Hall of Famer Larry Bird, also a Celtic, though one of slightly more renown. \u201cHe kept Stephen Jackson from beating me up.\u201d \u2014 Gregg Popovich , before Game 1 of the Golden State-San Antonio series, on the role assistant coach Mike Brown, now with the Warriors, played while he was an assistant with the Spurs. \u201cIt would be great to have a coach I could have for more than two years, you know.
