From Konstantin Epp : It\u2019s a fair question, Konstantin. I think we do tend to take people for granted when they\u2019ve maintained a state of excellence for as long as LeBron has. And I don\u2019t think there\u2019s any question it\u2019s probably cost him another MVP or two as voters (and I include myself in this) gravitate to the new thing. One could certainly cast an MVP vote for LeBron this year without hesitation. It would be hard for me to do so because the Cleveland Cavaliers have struggled so mightily for so many stretches during the season, especially at the defensive end, and LeBron was part of that struggle. In Westbrook\u2019s case, I think it\u2019s a combination of things. First, every season is its own and has its own drumbeat, so what voters thought last season doesn\u2019t matter this year. And that\u2019s why, while the Thunder\u2019s record this year is basically the same as last year, the circumstances of OKC\u2019s season are totally different. Last year, OKC was coming off the devastating loss of Kevin Durant, leaving the Thunder and Westbrook without a top-five player to play off of. That Westbrook not only led the team to the playoffs, but put up the numbers he did, was remarkable in that context. Yes, Victor Oladipo and Domantas Sabonis, in retrospect, were better than they showed there, but they never would have gotten the opportunity there that they have in Indiana. Maybe coach Billy Donovan could have and should have done more to incorporate them. It\u2019s a fair criticism. Second, it\u2019s so facile to dismiss Westbrook\u2019s triple-double accomplishments by saying he\u2019s hunting stats. Name me a superstar that doesn\u2019t, or hasn\u2019t, or who hasn\u2019t had assistance from time to time from sympathetic home stat crews. The bottom line is that no one had averaged a triple-double since Oscar Robertson in 1962. Westbrook has now done it two years in a row. To dismiss that is the height of arrogance in our hot-take world. But, it\u2019s also arrogant not to acknowledge the numbers James Harden has put up this season, the record that the Houston Rockets have and the way they\u2019ve played almost all year with Harden on the floor. Even the White Shadow had a probationary period. From Travis Stevens: Great question. To me, the biggest thing an NBA head coach has to have is credibility. How they are credible can take different forms.  A former player brings a certain gravitas to a locker room, to be sure. For example, if Kevin Garnett ever decided he wanted to be a coach, I think he\u2019d have a chance to be good at it; players respect him immensely. Other coaches gain credibility with their work ethic and\/or grind, like Erik Spoelstra or Stevens. Obviously, comfort with and belief in analytics, in today\u2019s NBA, is a must as well. Even when you\u2019re not winning, if you can show players why you want them to do something by being comfortable with modern numbers and what they mean \u2014 think Brett Brown \u2014 you can survive a bad year or two. The late Daly said it so well so many years ago: an NBA team is like 12 different Fortune 500 companies. They must allow you to coach them. As great as Popovich is, if Tim Duncan had tired of being cussed out in practice or in the film room, Pop would have never made it. It\u2019s not you, it\u2019s me. From Sherley Jean-Pierre: Has there been any thought given to awarding the No. 1 Draft pick to the non-playoff team with the best record? This would certainly create an incentive against tanking. Oh, there\u2019s been so much talk, Sherley. And I think that\u2019s part of the problem. Everyone seems to be so desperate to make the Lottery\/playoffs perfect that they\u2019re in danger of tinkering them to death (and I\u2019m not talking about your idea, which seems reasonable and logical). It\u2019s just that at some point the league and the teams need to come to an understanding that the Lottery is an imperfect system and that while it may be unfair to Western Conference teams that the balance of power is currently in that conference, there\u2019s no perfect solution that solves all potential problems. And the Hawks, who stripped Budenholzer of his team president title last summer, can go about the business of bringing in a new guy who fits what GM Travis Schlenk wants. 3) God speed, Jerry Reynolds , the longtime TV color analyst for the Kings, who\u2019s retiring after more than two decades with the franchise . There\u2019s so much wonderful prose in here, so much detail and so much love for the subject. My beloved alma mater has many great things going for it \u2014 world-class schools of international service and communication, extensive ties to the political and journalism worlds both locally and nationally. Oscar winners went to AU . Pulitzer Prize winners taught at AU . And, Judge Judy went there, too ! But \u2026 sports \u2026 not so much. AU\u2019s moments in the athletic sun are few and far between. Our soccer team reached the NCAA national championship game in 1985, but lost to UCLA, 1-0, in a game that ran eight overtimes. Eight. We currently play in the Patriot League, not a hotbed for notable sports figures \u2014 though C.J. McCollum (Lehigh) and Mike Muscala (Bucknell) have broken through in recent years, joining Adonal Foyle (Colgate) as Patriot League players turned NBA players. AU\u2019s only NBA player had been Kermit Washington , a rugged power forward in the 1970s who was the last NCAA player to average 20 points and 20 rebounds in one season. We love Kermit. Unfortunately, he is more infamous outside AU for hitting Rudy Tomjanovich during a fight in 1978 than famous for his good works with players as a trainer and union rep and charity work with kids in Africa. AU just doesn\u2019t register on that side of the athletic street. When one of our own has a magical, very good week, you can be I\u2019ll be next to the rooster, crowing in the dawn\u2019s early light. Step into the spotlight, Andre Ingram, Class of \u201907, physics major, part-time math tutor, all-time 3-point leader in the G League \u2014 and, finally, after 10 years in the minors, NBA rookie, at 32. It\u2019s so rare, in our hot-take, quick-to-outrage world, that there comes a story that everyone can feel good about and not carp about on Twitter. It\u2019s not that you\u2019re a cynic about these things, but people, being people, are human. They\u2019re fallible. They make mistakes in judgment that can be disappointing. But Ingram produces similar reactions in everyone \u2014 genuine joy at his accomplishment, given the work he put in over a decade to get here. Called up by the Lakers from their G League affiliate , the South Bay Lakers, to play the last two games of the NBA season, Ingram didn\u2019t just show up. He thrived as if channeling Roy Hobbs . Against the best team in the league, the Houston Rockets, he scored 19 points on 6 of 8 shooting , including 4 of 5 on 3-pointers. The next night, against the LA Clippers, he didn\u2019t shoot it as well, making just 2 of 9 shots. But he had six assists, three rebounds and two steals , finishing with a +23 in 35 minutes on the floor. And he handled all of it the same way he\u2019s handled everything since his days at AU (2003-07), where he\u2019s still fifth on the school\u2019s all-time scoring list and was a three-time all-Patriot League selection. \u201cI listened to myself when I was interviewed by somebody about him,\u201d said his coach at AU, Jeff Jones, on Sunday. \u201cI\u2019m sure there are people out there who will read it and go \u2018okay, right.\u2019 But it\u2019s one of those things. He\u2019s special \u2026 he\u2019s just one of those people who, he\u2019s a better person than the rest of us\u201d Jones, now the coach at Old Dominion, invited Ingram to work his camp last year to speak. \u201cOf course, he comes early,\u201d Jones said. And afterward, Ingram didn\u2019t want to be paid for his time. \u201cWhen we met with Andre, he\u2019s a person that\u2019s kind of represented by gratitude and appreciation,\u201d Lakers General Manager Rob Pelinka said Saturday. \u201cBeing in the room when we kind of surprised him \u2014 he thought it was an exit meeting \u2014 and we made an offer to wear the Lakers jersey, you could just see the emotion hit him, and then when he called his family. He\u2019s a terrific person. And in the building, all of a sudden, we\u2019re playing against the Rockets, and our crowd is chanting MVP when Andre Ingram\u2019s at the line. It as kind of a surreal moment.\u201d Ingram has carried himself the same way for years \u2014 dedicated to his craft, but also to helping when he can. That\u2019s taken many forms, including tutoring kids in math to supplement his basketball salaries and his young family, and being a mentor to his young South Bay teammates. \u201cThe value of him off the court for our G League team is something that has to be mentioned,\u201d South Bay General Manager Nick Mazzella said by phone Sunday. \u201cWe rely on developing young players. To have a guy in the locker room who can put an arm around a young guy going through a tough stretch, to show them good practice habits, that\u2019s invaluable to us.\u201d He was a star the moment he stepped on campus at AU, earning Patriot League Freshman of the Year honors, helping the Eagles reach the Patriot League championship game in 2004. He shouldn\u2019t have ever wound up there, having starred for Highland Springs High in Richmond. But Ingram was one of those talents that, for some reason, was barely recruited. \u201cVCU was his local team,\u201d Jones said. \u201cHe wasn\u2019t recruited there, or (the University of) Richmond. We knew. We absolutely knew that we were getting a steal. This was a kid that was better than his recruitment \u2026 we were holding our breath the hold time. He played AAU the summer before, because he was young. He was playing, maybe, in Orlando. And I was down there, and I was worried. He was averaging 30 a game. And all it took was somebody to persuade him to play at Fork Union for a (postgraduate) year, and then our NLI (National Letter of Intent) would be over. We never did talk about it. And I mentioned it this summer and he said \u2018I wasn\u2019t going to do that.\u2019\u201d He played the exact same way he does now \u2014 no wasted motion on the floor, constantly finding the soft spots in opponents\u2019 defenses, leading by example. But Ingram went undrafted in \u201907. He felt he could get to the NBA, though, and started the grind, beginning with the Utah Flash , which selected him in the seventh round of what was then the D-League Draft. After four years there, Ingram was the franchise\u2019s all-time leading scorer, making 328 3-pointers. (The Flash ceased operations in 2011 and was resurrected across the country two years later in Delaware; the team is now the Philadelphia 76ers\u2019 G League affiliate, the Delaware Blue Coats .) Ingram then started with the Lakers\u2019 organization, with the D-Fenders, always grinding toward his ultimate goal of making the NBA. As he kept making 3-pointers, and the NBA started turning toward small ball and wings who could shoot it from deep, a callup seemed in the cards. But it never materialized. A brief stop in Australia in 2016 lasted all of two games, and it was back to the G League and the Lakers, whose affiliate was relocated and renamed. As Ingram\u2019s hair turned gray, he kept making 3-pointers with that funky, Michael Adams-like release , becoming the G League\u2019s all-time leader in made 3-pointers. He was a testament to getting in the lab and perfecting the craft . But no call came from the parent team, which was dealing with the death of patriarch Jerry Buss in 2013 , a cascading series of bad personnel moves and the end of Kobe Bryant\u2019s career. There was just too much going on. But Jeanie Buss assumed control of the team last year, firing her brother Jim Buss and general manager Mitch Kupchak , and chose Magic Johnson and Pelinka to run the franchise. The Lakers vowed to start doing things differently and be more patient, building through the Draft and giving Luke Walton a real shot to grow into his role as coach. When the end of the Lakers\u2019 2017-18 regular season approached, with several players out with injuries, the team made one of its longest-serving employees\u2019 dreams come to fruition. \u201cA lot of the credit on this goes to our head of South Bay, Joey Buss,\u201d Pelinka said. \u201cThey approached Earvin and I with the concept. It\u2019s interesting; Jeanie Buss\u2019 driving philosophy is under this new leadership we have, is to do what\u2019s right for people, and always make the best choice. There\u2019s been times this year when we had to do that, and it was hard, like with Corey Brewer. We knew he wasn\u2019t playing and we could let him go and he could be on a playoff team ( Brewer signed with Oklahoma City after the Lakers released him ). If you do enough of the right things for people, it kind of comes back into how your franchise and your culture is viewed.\u201d It was several weeks in the making. \u201cThroughout the year, Joey Buss and I would meet with Rob and Magic and Luke about guys in the G League that we thought could be potential callups, guys that we would recommend,\u201d Mazzella said. \u201cAndre was a name that always came up. As much as it\u2019s a feel-good story, he plays the game the right way. He\u2019s the best shooter in the league. He\u2019s an underrated defender.\u201d The Lakers tapped into the G League throughout the season, bringing Nigel Hayes in from Westchester in February, and Travis Wear and Alex Caruso from South Bay. Finally, after a rash of injuries hit the parent team after the All-Star break \u2014 Josh Hart broke his hand; Brandon Ingram strained his groin; Kyle Kuzma banged up both ankles; Kentavious Caldwell-Pope sprained his right shoulder, and on and on \u2014 it was Andre Ingram\u2019s moment. Walton, who had been an assistant on the D-Fenders before going to Golden State Warriors to join Steve Kerr\u2019s staff, was all for it. South Bay and the Lakers both practiced in the franchise\u2019s new UCLA Health Training Center. All the coaches knew each other. It was like having a 27-man roster, basically. The conceit was that Ingram was being brought in for his exit interview after South Bay\u2019s season ended in the G League semifinals , and that everyone\u2019s interviews were being filmed for an end-of-season videography. \u201cWe wanted him to be the first to know,\u201d Mazzella said. \u201cIt would be great for Andre to show his family. And, it would be great for South Bay Lakers Twitter.\u201d No one expected what happened next. Something in Ingram\u2019s story touched people \u2014 not just NBA fans, but fans of anyone who doesn\u2019t give up on their dream. \u201cObviously, I was happy,\u201d said Ingram\u2019s business manager, Romone Penny \u2014 also a former AU player. \u201cAnd then I said, all this boy needs is a few minutes, and he\u2019s going to shine \u2014 he\u2019s going to show them what he can do. He\u2019s going to show them that he belongs.\u201d Which is when this got ridiculous. \u201cAndre took it and made into something even bigger,\u201d Pelinka said, which is about when The New York Times , ESPN , \u201cGood Morning America\u201d , the Los Angeles Dodgers and 96 other people that have blown up Penny\u2019s phone in the last week came calling. Which is why Ingram retreated to Virginia this weekend for some R and R before he starts to think about what\u2019s next. It\u2019s not easy to answer once the glow of last week starts to wear off. Here stands a 32-year-old rookie with exactly two games of NBA experience under his belt. That\u2019s not disqualifying by any means, but it\u2019s not exactly the resume of a Lottery pick. Ingram could go back to South Bay, one supposes. But once they\u2019ve seen the lights of Broadway, it\u2019s hard to go back to Summer Stock, not that there\u2019s anything wrong with Shakespeare in the Park. Could the Lakers have two Ingrams \u2014 Andre and Brandon \u2014 in camp next fall? \u201cThe other big philosophy of Jeanie Buss is once a Laker, always a Laker,\u201d Pelinka said. \u201cWe had our (real) exit meetings and we just said \u2018we want you around here. We want you in our building, continuing to work. Your spirit is so inspirational to all of us, the way you work.\u2019 We\u2019ll kind of let it unfold and see how happens, but definitely want him to stay close.\u201d I\u2019m too old to believe in fairy tales. But Andre Ingram can play somewhere in the NBA in 2018-19. Maybe it\u2019s not with the Lakers, who have a lot of young mouths to feed and dreams of bringing in some big-time free agents in July. But you can\u2019t tell me there isn\u2019t room on someone\u2019s roster somewhere for a no-excuses, hard-working, great teammate who may come into a came and drop five threes on a team that doesn\u2019t close out on him hard. I\u2019d believe that even if we hadn\u2019t gone to AU a decade apart. But it makes believing easier. \u201cHe showed at 32 years old, he fits,\u201d Mazzella said. \u201cEveryone\u2019s career path is different. He\u2019s the first guy in the weight room. He\u2019s always taking care of himself. I don\u2019t look at him as a guy past his prime getting a chance at the end. I think Andre still has something to prove.\u201d * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. That first step, that first foray into the great postseason abyss , required this sort of confirmation from the No. 1 overall seed in the entire tournament, so to speak. There\u2019s no room for Cinderellas around here, no slaying of Goliath, not on Clint Capela\u2019s watch. Not with James Harden on the case when the Rockets needed it most, and especially at crunch time. And not with Chris Paul, chip planted firmly on his shoulder as always, eyeballing bigger and better things than being the best from late October to mid-April. So it won\u2019t be easy. Nobody said it would be. And let\u2019s be clear, the Minnesota Timberwolves are not a normal eight seed. Not really. A healthy Jimmy Butler and the infusion of veteran talent that helped end the second longest playoff drought in NBA history this season makes that big a difference. They certainly did Sunday night at Toyota Center, when the Rockets were forced to battle until the very end for a 104-101 win despite a 44-point masterpiece from Harden. But like everyone else who dealt with these juggernaut Rockets all season long, Harden and his crew proved to be too much with the game on the line. With Harden on the bench and the game tied at 85 with 6:49 to play, the script was already written. He came in for Paul with 6:07 to play and the Rockets up a point, and promptly scored on a driving layup. He stole the ball and then scored on a driving floater. After a Capela block, he scored on a driving layup. By the time he knocked down a 3-pointer with 4:27 left, the Rockets\u2019 lead was back up to eight points, 94-86, and it was clear that Harden was going to do whatever it took \u2014 scoring, playmaking and even defending \u2014 to keep Game 1 from going awry. It was vintage work from the maestro who has owned the floor most every night since the season opener, when Harden and the Rockets went into Oracle Arena as the reigning champion Golden State Warriors hung another banner and collected those diamond-laced title rings and walked off the floor winners. \u201cAnother day for James,\u201d Rockets coach Mike D\u2019Antoni said after Harden finished one point shy of his playoff career high. \u201cHe\u2019s done it all year and he really stepped up. We were struggling to make shots, struggling to really have any kind of rhythm of play and James put us on his back and he\u2019s been doing it for a while now.\u201d D\u2019Antoni will have to forgive the rest of us, including the frontrunner for the Kia MVP this season, for not digesting his theory about the playoffs being something other than a referendum on his team\u2019s magical regular season. Harden operated like someone keenly aware of what was at stake with the Timberwolves, each and every one of them, trying in vain to slow him down. \u201cHonestly, I just try to be aggressive and make the right play,\u201d Harden said. \u201cThings got slowed up a little bit, just try to be aggressive with my shot and fortunately it went in.\u201d Jimmy Butler is an All-Star and one of the league\u2019s best two-way players. Derrick Rose is a former Kia MVP himself, and still has enough juice left to make things difficult for someone when he locks in the way he did on this night. And neither one of them had any luck slowing Harden down during his second-half blitz. He scored 25 of his points in the final 18 minutes, making play after play when the Timberwolves appeared to be on the verge of potentially pulling off a shocker. \u201cThere were several plays in which I thought we defended well and he made shots,\u201d Timberwolves coach Tom Thibodeau said. \u201cJames is that type of player and we\u2019ve seen it all year, (he\u2019s) very difficult to guard. Basically, you have to guard him with your whole team. And it\u2019s not just his scoring, but his playmaking and all the things that he does.\u201d The Rockets won on a night when they shot a brutal 27 percent (10-for-37) from beyond the 3-point line, where they\u2019ve feasted on the opposition all season. They roasted the Timberwolves from distance during their regular season match ups to the tune of 43.4 percent and more than doubled them up in 3-point makes during those games, but made just two more Sunday night. Harden was 7-for-12 from deep, a playoff career-high for makes, while the rest of the Rockets shot a combined 3-for-25. And he was draining his shots with hands in his face routinely. \u201cHe\u2019s an MVP candidate and you know why,\u201d said Timberwolves big man Taj Gibson. \u201cEvery time the game was \u2018mono e mono\u2019 and they were in a tight spot, he just took over the game. He made some tough shots, he played phenomenal tonight. We were trying to throw everything at him, he\u2019s a talented player.\u201d He\u2019s clearly much more than that. \u201cI mean yeah, he\u2019s a hell of a player,\u201d Butler said. \u201cEveryone knows that. But you don\u2019t just guard him with one guy. It\u2019s everybody out there, everybody has to be in the correct position. Challenge shots; contest them at the rim, but more than anything, if there is a miss we\u2019ve got to get the rebound and take off the other way. But we didn\u2019t do any of that tonight, we\u2019ve got to be better (in Game 2) on Wednesday.\u201d Thibodeau had to turn to his bench to stay in the game before halftime and they delivered, scoring 19 points and playing with an energy level that matched what the Rockets did regardless of who was on the floor. Rose (nine points), Jamal Crawford (seven) and Gorgui Dieng (three) did all that bench scoring, which was the only way to offset the furious 49 points Capela and Harden combined for before the break. Jeff Teague\u2019s three fouls and Butler\u2019s defensive task, trying to keep Harden under wraps, required so much of his attention that the scoring load had to be picked up by someone else. He went scoreless in the first quarter and just never seemed to get untracked early on, finishing with just 13 points on 4-for-11 shooting. It\u2019s an issue the Timberwolves won\u2019t be able to scheme their way out of in this series, not as long as Capela is the most energetic and effective young big man on either team. He outscored the All-Star Towns 20-3 before the break and out rebounded him 10-5, adding two blocks and a steal to drive home the point that he\u2019s up for this challenge all series long. \u201cMan, Clint was all over the place, both ends of the court offensively and defensively,\u201d Paul said. \u201cYou see him defending KAT, who\u2019s a tough cover in the post. You know I\u2019m low, and I weak side and I\u2019m watching him go up for the hook, and then I\u2019m watching Clint block it, and then he\u2019s running. he was unbelievable tonight and we\u2019re going to need that all season.\u201d Capela finished his night with 24 points, 12 rebounds and three blocks while Towns didn\u2019t crack double digits in the scoring column (eight points on 3-for-9 shooting, 12 rebounds in a team-high 40 minutes of action). Chalk it up as a lesson learned for the playoff rookie. That must-win game the Timberwolves won at home over Denver Wednesday night had all the hype and intensity of a playoff game, only it wasn\u2019t. Thibodeau credited the Rockets\u2019 defense, the swarming and double-teaming of Towns, for slowing the big man down. \u201cHe has to be more active,\u201d Thibodeau said, before praising the Rockets for perhaps their most underrated trait this season: The ability to lock down defensively. \u201cThey\u2019re good, they\u2019re very good. They\u2019re tied together, they do a lot of switching and after the switch they read the ball extremely well. They react, they swarm, and so you have ti make good decisions, you have to make good plays. You have to have the ability to read and react.\u201d Funny, that\u2019s what the Rockets\u2019 best player does perhaps as well as any other player in the league right now. Harden reads and reacts accordingly, always seemingly coming up with the right play at the right time. That\u2019s how you know he\u2019s in the moment right now, as are the rest of the Rockets. No matter how many times and how many different ways anyone tries to deflect attention from the obvious, they comprehend every bit of what lies ahead for a team riding into the postseason on the strength of a 65-win regular season that saw them run away from the competition. They wouldn\u2019t have souls if they didn\u2019t. They wouldn\u2019t be human if they hadn\u2019t already calculated the weight of the best regular season in franchise history times a wide-open postseason equaling something that\u2019s never been done here, which says a lot for a franchise that has two Larry O\u2019Brien trophies to show off. They know how important each and every step on this current journey is, starting with Sunday night\u2019s very first choppy ones. Any suggestion to the contrary is, shall we say, a distant cousin of the truth. But we\u2019ll play along for now, at the beginning. Sekou Smith is a veteran NBA reporter and NBA TV analyst. They limited the Cavaliers to 80 points and 8-of-34 3-point shooting. They dictated the pace, controlled the ball and led 25-8 late in the first quarter and 33-14 when it ended. The Pacers stymied a couple Cleveland players with their defensive matchups (notably sticking big man Myles Turner on Jeff Green), exploited the playoff inexperience of some of the Cavs\u2019 newcomers and created points by creating turnovers (12 steals). It\u2019s just that it hasn\u2019t been magnified. It\u2019s the playoffs now \u2026 so everybody sees it. So, it\u2019s like, it\u2019s kind of shocking to everybody.\u201d Oladipo scored 32 points on 11-of-19 shooting, 6-of-9 from the arc, and added six rebounds, four assists and four steals. That put James at point guard even longer, keeping him out of the post where he looked capable of doing more damage, while limiting his downhill assaults on the rim. Not every one of Cleveland\u2019s 26 missed 3-pointer was hotly contested but few were wide-open looks either. \u201cThey came in and they dictated the tempo,\u201d said James, who got a triple-double with 24 points, 10 rebounds and 12 assists but was a minus-13 in his 44 minutes. \u201cThey were more aggressive. They just played inspired basketball. \u2026 They were more physical than us at the point of attack. They were more precise in what they wanted to do. And we couldn\u2019t score the ball.\u201d James was anything but flummoxed (\u201cI\u2019m down 0-1 in the first round. I was down 3-1 in the Finals\u201d). You\u2019re on their home court. You\u2019re going to have to show that calmness. Essentially, they\u2019re trailing the Golden State Warriors and also failing in their attempt to get their franchise player back in uniform and in time to save their season. And unless something drastic happens in both situations, the Spurs look destined to go 0-for-2. Being down 1-0 in the best-of-seven to the Warriors is easy to explain. Golden State has Kevin Durant, Klay Thompson, Draymond Green and a determination to repeat as NBA champions, so they\u2019re strong out of the gate in these playoffs and are anxiously pouncing on the disadvantaged Spurs. That\u2019s evident. After looking lethargic and uninterested over the last few weeks of the regular season, and perhaps understandably since there was nothing to play for in particular, the Warriors are ready to wake up. The other contest is more complex and head-scratching for the Spurs. Clearly, there\u2019s friction or at least a major wedge between Kawhi Leonard and the Spurs although nobody knows how it arrived to this point and the extent of the damage and where it\u2019s going. Or maybe, nobody\u2019s willing or able to explain. It\u2019s not even evident that Kawhi is the problem, or that he has a personal beef with coach Gregg Popovich and the organization, since he doesn\u2019t talk publicly or reveals much when he does. Yes, it\u2019s complicated. Here\u2019s the facts: Leonard has played only nine games this season, citing a quad injury which normally needs a half-season to heal properly based on similar injuries. While the Spurs are here preparing for Game 2 on Monday, he\u2019s in New York doing rehab work. The Spurs medical staff cleared him two months ago but Leonard sought a second opinion and his experts haven\u2019t given the green light, so he\u2019s working out on his own. Also, Leonard has one year left on his current contract and, because he made the All-NBA team last season, he qualifies for the \u201csuper max\u201d extension which the Spurs can give him this summer. Here\u2019s what\u2019s fuzzy: Leonard\u2019s communication with Popovich and the staff has, by all indications, been professional and pure if limited and murky. That\u2019s because Leonard\u2019s inner circle of family members aren\u2019t on the same page, it seems, with the Spurs. And his future as the successor to David Robinson and Tim Duncan in San Antonio, which once seemed like a layup, isn\u2019t so automatic anymore. That\u2019s what led Popovich to say this, on the eve of Game 2, regarding Leonard\u2019s status for the Warriors: \u201cYou\u2019ll have to ask Kawhi and his group that question.\u201d And this: \u201cSo far they say that he\u2019s not ready to go. So, we can\u2019t do anything until that happens, then we would have to decide what\u2019s going on from there.\u201d They. His group. We. Not exactly a response or words that projects harmony and understanding and a kinship. A report by Yahoo! Sports on Sunday revealed that Leonard will not be available for the rest of the playoffs. What\u2019s weird is Leonard was exactly the type of superstar the Spurs were accustomed to having: Someone quiet, who didn\u2019t care about his \u201cbrand,\u201d unselfish with teammates, coachable and respectful to the demanding Popovich, excellent all-around on the floor, rose to the occasion in the post-season and single-minded in a pursuit of a championship. Also, just a few weeks ago Popovich refused to question Leonard\u2019s desire to resume playing; after all, Leonard was a Finals MVP and never hid his desire to compete. What happened? How much of this situation is due to Leonard\u2019s contract extension? Usually, money plays a role in these matters, either a sneaky one or obvious one. Also, there\u2019s no guarantee that Popovich will get the OK from ownership to pay a super max in excess of $200 million to Leonard after such a weird and unsettled season. Even before money is discussed, the Spurs must ask him and \u201chis people\u201d this fundamental and direct question this summer: Do you want to be here? If so, the two sides must begin to lay out the ground rules and forge a better understanding about his injury, and also map out his financial future. If not, then the Lakers and Clippers (Leonard is from LA) will likely receive a call, among others. His value is obvious; a pesty and aggressive 6-7 swingman, Leonard might be the best man-to-man defender in basketball and improved his shooting range enough to be a top-10 scorer. But that\u2019s an issue for July. In the meantime, here in the playoffs, Leonard is the most important player and he hasn\u2019t broken a sweat. The Spurs are undermanned against the biggest force of the last five years, and that could make for a quick series. \u201cThey\u2019re missing No. 2,\u201d said Durant, citing Leonard\u2019s jersey number. \u201cSo yeah, they look different from that aspect. They play the same way and it\u2019s always about the game. All the rest of that stuff doesn\u2019t matter to Pop and the rest of the players. That\u2019s why they\u2019ve had so much success over the years. And they play a good brand. They believe in each other and they don\u2019t beat themselves. It\u2019s a good combination to have but obviously they\u2019re missing their best player and we\u2019re just trying to take advantage of it.\u201d Without Leonard, there\u2019s no second source of offense next to LaMarcus Aldridge and the Spurs are leaning on past-their-prime players such as Rudy Gay, Manu Ginobili and Tony Parker. Without Leonard, Thompson shot 11 of 13 in Game 1 and had a number of open looks that he conceded he normally doesn\u2019t get against the Spurs. \u201cIt\u2019s a big difference,\u201d Thompson said. \u201cKawhi is MVP caliber. Not having him affects them drastically. It was always fun because he was one of the best defenders in the game. I took it as a challenge to have a great game with Kawhi covering me, knowing what he brings and the energy he uses on every possession. He\u2019s an amazing player on both sides of the ball.\u201d But right now, he\u2019s a ghost, a rumor, a person of the past and a non-factor when it matters most. That\u2019s never been said before about Kawhi Leonard and the post-season. Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. (We\u2019ll say that the Cleveland Cavaliers of the last two seasons are the exception to the rule, though it must be acknowledged that their championship season was the one in which they ranked in the top 10 defensively.) The Philadelphia 76ers, just two years after completing a season in which they lost 72 games, have become great on both ends of the floor. The Sixers ranked third defensively this season. J.J. Redick is their only starter shorter than 6-9 and size matters on that end of the floor. Ben Simmons was the rare rookie who made a positive impact defensively. But Game 1 of their first-round series against the Miami Heat, in addition to being the Sixers\u2019 17th straight win and their 24th win in 25 home games since Jan. 1, was the latest example of how big of a leap they\u2019ve made offensively over the last two months. The Sixers scored 130 points on just 98 possessions on Saturday, chewing up what was the seventh-ranked defense in the regular season. The 18-for-28 from 3-point range that the Sixers shot in the 130-103 victory is not sustainable. But even if all those shots aren\u2019t dropping, they won\u2019t be significantly easier to guard in Game 2 on Monday. \u201cThey play really, really fast,\u201d Heat center Kelly Olynyk said after his team allowed 74 points in the second half. \u201cA lot of stuff in transition. They just kept those shooters coming off staggered screens, non-stop, full speed and it\u2019s tough to guard when they got four shooters out there with Simmons handling the ball it\u2019s tough to help and pack the paint, get in the paint. \u201cThey\u2019re coming off making tough shots, throwing the ball back, other guys are making shots, Simmons is attacking, they\u2019re getting back cuts, trying to guard off screens. They play really fast, they execute really, really well.\u201d The Sixers have played faster since losing Joel Embiid to a facial fracture in late March. But they\u2019ve been playing with pace, moving bodies and moving the ball since the start of \u201cThe Process.\u201d This is the system that head coach Brett Brown has been developing for the last five years. For most of that time, he just didn\u2019t have the personnel to execute it well. But all those seasons at the bottom of the standings have resulted in an influx of talent. More talent has resulted in better execution, and suddenly, this team has become hard to stop. Without Embiid, Simmons is driving the bus. But a playmaker, no matter how talented, needs the right people around him. And as the Cavs and Golden State Warriors have taught us over the last few years, you can never have too much shooting in this league. Redick, sixth among active players in career 3-point percentage, was the Sixers\u2019 big addition in free agency last summer. Dario Saric was the most improved 3-point shooter in the league this season, and Robert Covington led the league in catch-and-shoot 3s. But it wasn\u2019t until February, when the Sixers added both Marco Belinelli and Ersan Ilyasova in the buyout market, that the offense really took off. When the Sixers signed Belinelli on Feb. 12, they ranked 14th in offensive efficiency, having scored 105 points per 100 possessions over their first 53 games. They had seen a big improvement from last season (when they ranked last offensively for the fourth straight year), but were still worse than average on that end of the floor. After the Belinelli addition (Ilyasova would sign two weeks later), they ranked fifth on offense, scoring more than 111 points per 100 possessions over their final 29 games. The Sixers were the only team that ranked in the top five in both offensive and defensive efficiency after the All-Star break. The defense has been there all season, and it helped the Sixers take the lead for good on Saturday with a 15-3 run early in the third quarter by holding the Heat scoreless on eight straight possessions. The offense, or at least its foundation, has been there all season too. The Sixers led the league in ball movement (passes per minute of possession) and ranked second in player movement (miles traveled per minute of possession). Last season, they led the league in both. This is who they are and who\u2019ve they\u2019ve been since Day 1 with Brown. \u201cWhat we did the first day of (training) camp is what we needed to win tonight,\u201d Brown said Saturday. \u201cPassing the ball, sharing the ball, playing downhill, understanding it\u2019s really the magic play in the playoffs. It\u2019s always about concepts and spacing and unselfish players.\u201d The Sixers recorded assists on 34 of their 45 baskets in Game 1. Belinelli, Ilyasova and Redick combined to score 70 points and that game-deciding run came with with Ilyasova \u2013 one more shooter around Simmons \u2013 starting the third quarter in place of Amir Johnson. Pace, space, ball movement, player movement, and lights-out shooting \u2014 it all came together in an incredible offensive display. But Brown began planning for this moment five years ago. And when he finally got the players talented enough to make his system work, he was ready to plug them in. \u201cWhether Ersan comes in or Marco comes in, that\u2019s who we are,\u201d Brown said. \u201cThat\u2019s what we\u2019re trying to coach here. And the guys have bought in. We play fast, we look for each other. The 3-point shot, without Joel, has become a real big part of our identity. We\u2019ll continue to do that. You have to do that or you won\u2019t win games when it matters.\u201d For the first time in six years, the Philadelphia 76ers have won a game that matters. And with how good they\u2019ve become on both ends of the floor, there will be more to come. John Schuhmann is a staff writer for NBA.com. Just not this team from Texas. No, not the Spurs, at least that\u2019s what the hunch and the outset of this first-round playoff series says. Common sense, too, wants to chime in and declare the Spurs without Kawhi Leonard are just standing in the way, albeit proudly, of the Warriors\u2019 path to greater things in the post-season. And so, the long and antsy wait for the anticipated and projected Warriors vs. Houston Rockets showdown in the West began in earnest Saturday when the Warriors breezed from jump ball to buzzer against San Antonio, and also torpedoed the notion that they\u2019re somehow vulnerable (at least for now). The playoff tipoff was all about the Warriors re-establishing their muscle flex and their defense and most importantly, their aura, even with Steph Curry still out and ailing, because the rest of the NBA was watching. That\u2019s what a 113-92 wipeout Game 1 win at Oracle Arena accomplished, if nothing else. The combination of the Spurs being overmatched and the Warriors needing to put on a more menacing face than what they showed the last month of the regular season delivered the predictable result: A smackdown. Or, as Spurs coach Gregg Popovich put it: \u201cGot our ass kicked.\u201d Too much Kevin Durant, the sneaky elevation of Andre Iguodala to the starting point guard spot, a JaVale McGee sighting and Klay Thompson getting swishy was exactly what the Warriors needed and received. Seriously, though: Anything less would\u2019ve been a big surprise. The Warriors couldn\u2019t afford to stay locked into the season-ending fog that turned coach Steve Kerr\u2019s hair a lighter shade of gray and created the perception of a fat, too-satisfied winner of two championships in three years. Most likely, they were merely victims of human nature: While going 7-10 down the stretch , the Warriors simply grew bored with the meaningless late season, especially once Curry hurt his knee on March 23 . Seriously, what was left to accomplish, other than to stay healthy? This team was created and molded for the sole purpose of winning in June, not for placing importance on, say, drop-kicking the Sacramento Kings on April Fools. \u201cWe\u2019re a championship ballclub and we know what it takes to win this time of year,\u201d said Draymond Green, applying the perspective. \u201cYou heard, \u2018The Warriors lost it, they\u2019re not together, they\u2019re not the same team without Steph, blase, blase, blase, blah blah blah.\u2019 Well, we know what we\u2019re capable of. There have been series where we\u2019ve won without Kevin, without myself, without our head coach. A lot of people forgot.\u201d In case you\u2019re one of the people Draymond was referring to, here\u2019s a refresher course, courtesy of Game 1: Durant is very long and tough to defend, Thompson usually doesn\u2019t miss when he has three feet of separation from his defender, Iggy always earns his fat paycheck in springtime, and as for McGee?  \u201cHe\u2019s very tall,\u201d said Kerr. Actually, Kerr wasn\u2019t purposely trying to troll his starting center, just stating the obvious when it comes to defending Spurs leading scorer LaMarcus Aldridge. McGee brings four more inches and therefore made it tough for Aldridge, who managed only 14 points and was mainly a ghost. With Aldridge on lockdown, the Warriors\u2019 D had accomplished its main mission, because the Spurs lack a secondary source of punch. What, is Rudy Gay going to turn back the clock? Manu Ginobili? Tony Parker? Because that\u2019s what needs to happen for San Antonio. Without it, well, unfortunately for the Spurs, Kawhi wasn\u2019t limping through that door Sunday. What irritated Kerr was how the Warriors dialed down their defensive intensity in the weeks leading into the playoffs. They spotted 126 points each to the Pelicans and Pacers , and in the season final did a complete no-show, getting spanked by 40 courtesy of Utah . Remember, the Warriors constantly ranked among the better defensive teams during their multiple runs to the NBA Finals. As coaches tend to do in these matters, Kerr jeopardized his vocal chords a few times while trying to get the message across in the disinterested locker room. But deep down, did anyone buy the notion of the Warriors suddenly forgetting how to play defense? With the second-best record in the West secured, and first place conceded to Houston, weren\u2019t they just tapping their toes until the first round? Is that such a crime? Wouldn\u2019t that be understandable, and wise on some levels, given the risk of something bad happening to a hamstring? Anyway, Kerr barely uncrossed his legs on the bench Sunday; no need to scold a team that held the Spurs to 40 percent shooting and claimed the rebounding edge by 21 and never felt threatened. \u201cWe finally got back to defending,\u201d said Kerr. He made sure of that, by inserting Iguodala, his best all-around defender, into the starting lineup and also using McGee extensively instead of Zaza Pachulia. Iggy spread his limbs all over the floor, guarding bigs and smalls, switching on the screen and generally being a pest to the other team, as he generally is this time of year. \u201cI just wanted to put our best defensive lineup on the floor from the beginning,\u201d he explained. \u201cThe last month or so our defense had been subpar. You can\u2019t win in this league unless you defend. You could feel the intensity right from the start. We set a good tone.\u201d To be fair, the awakening of the Warriors\u2019 defense will receive a more accurate measure if and when they advance beyond the Spurs and face, for example, the Blazers with Damian Lillard and CJ McCollum and of course the turbocharged Rockets with James Harden and Chris Paul and all those three-point slingers. But until then, wrapping up the Spurs will serve as necessary preparation. Less worry was the Warriors on the other end of the floor, where Durant assumed the lead and took 17 shots, scoring 24 points. More efficient was Thompson, who missed only twice in 13 shots and finished with 27 points. All of this was necessary with Curry not expected back for the first round; he just received the green light to press the accelerator during rehab and begin lateral movements. If the Warriors, a top-10 team in both offensive and defensive efficiency, continue to get this kind of killer balance, there\u2019ll be some long nights upcoming for the Spurs and a quick series as well. \u201cWe were not as ready to face a team like them,\u201d said Ginobili. \u201cThey were much better than us. Klay got away from us many times. Overall, they were so much better in every aspect that we had no shot. We\u2019ve got to regroup, feel hurt and desperate, got to be smarter \u2026 We understand we\u2019re not favorites. We\u2019re underdogs. To get a win here we\u2019ve got to overachieve. We got to do better than we can.\u201d It\u2019s too bad that the Kawhi Situation continues to follow the Spurs like a dark cloud. He remains stymied by a quad injury that apparently hasn\u2019t healed enough for his liking. The Spurs with Kawhi and the Warriors without Curry would tip the scales in this series toward being somewhat level, or at least invite some suspense. Without Kawhi, the Spurs are shooting spitballs at a tank. Guts and hard work and good coaching can only go so far against the suddenly-refocused and playoff-locked Warriors. So, yes, the Warriors set out to re-introduce themselves and did so Sunday. \u201cWe want to make a statement in Game 2 as well,\u201d said Thompson. \u201cWe hobbled to the playoffs but we know how good we are and what it takes in the post season to win. When our intensity and focus are high, we\u2019re tough to beat.\u201d Well, tough for one team in Texas. We\u2019ll see about the other soon enough. Originally published Apr 13, 2018 9:47 PM ET"},{"id":40880,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/can-nba-postseason-format-see-changes-following-end-season-excitement","date":"2018-04-14","title":"Will end-of-season excitement spur postseason format change?","content":"NEW YORK \u2013 The fluke of what turned into an unplanned but highly watchable \u201cplay-in game\u201d Wednesday night, with Minnesota and Denver pitted in a win-or-go-fishing challenge to crack the NBA\u2019s field of 16 postseason teams, generated discussion at the Board of Governors meeting this week. Might the league add the concept to its playoff format, formalizing a one-and-done round the way major league baseball did in 2012 with the addition of its wild-card games? \u201cIt continued interest,\u201d NBA commissioner Adam Silver said at a news conference Friday evening. \u201cThere have been proposals that the league has been looking at over the last several weeks.\u201d Silver said the idea has been raised before with the league\u2019s Competition Committee and discussed at a previous Board of Governors meeting. He said he expects it will come up again when the franchise representatives meet again in July in Las Vegas. A play-in round of short series or single-game elimination has been rattling around the NHL for a while, generating support from some of hockey\u2019s general managers . But the NBA, like the NHL, already qualifies more than half its teams for the playoffs. Baseball, even with the added layer of wild-card games, still has only 10 out of 30 teams involved in postseason play. There also is a feeling among some MLB critics that a single game at the end of the 162-game regular season diminishes the accomplishment of the three division winners in the National and American Leagues. What played out between the Timberwolves and the Nuggets was organic, the result of the two Northwest Division teams tying with 46-35 records through their first 81 games. They just happened to be scheduled to meet in the season finale for both clubs, and the overtime thriller \u2013 with Minnesota ending the league\u2019s longest-active playoff drought (2004) by winning, 112-106 \u2013 was a highlight on a night that already offered the drama of first-round matchups being determined on the fly. Whether orchestrating an annual event pitting, say, the eighth- and ninth-place teams in each conference and matching the enthusiasm and TV audience of Wolves-Nuggets would be in question. But Silver sounded game. \u201cThe answer is \u2018yes\u2019 for me,\u201d the commissioner said. \u201cIt was an incredibly exciting game and it does open up those possibilities that we could do something more formalized, but certainly a play-in type of format.\u201d Being on the brink of the 2018 playoffs put a couple other topics in play both at the Governors meeting and at Silver\u2019s media session. Three of the East playoff teams \u2013 the Heat and the Bucks at 44-38, the Wizards at 43-39 \u2013 remain alive even as the 46-36 Nuggets go home. But talk of qualifying the teams with the 16 best records regardless of conference, or of seeding the eight East and eight West clubs 1-through-16, remains just that: talk. \u201cI think we\u2019re at the point right now where nobody has a better solution,\u201d Silver said. \u201cAs we\u2019ve talked about before, it\u2019s a balance of travel issues vs. conference issues vs. how the schedule should be set. \u2026 We\u2019re satisfied with where we are, because when you look at the alternative, the travel becomes overwhelming.\u201d Also, with the NBA\u2019s most emotional time of year coming in the form of 15 best-of-seven series between now and the middle of June, Silver gave an update on the player-referee friction that drew attention earlier this season. The referees and the players held a small \u201csummit\u201d meeting at All-Star Weekend in Los Angeles in February, while the league tackled the issue on a separate front. Michelle Johnson, NBA senior VP, head of referee operations, and longtime game official Monty McCutchen, in his new role as head of referee development and training, have visited with players and coaches from each of the 30 teams in a series of \u201crespect for the game\u201d meetings, accompanied by other league executives. \u201cThe reports back I\u2019ve gotten, both from Monty and Michelle and directly from the teams, is that they\u2019ve been very constructive,\u201d Silver said, \u201cand they\u2019ve been very appreciative that we\u2019ve taken the time to have those discussions and, again, to clarify some of the misunderstandings. But ultimately, I don\u2019t think there are any shortcuts.\u201d Conversations with Michele Roberts, executive director of the National Basketball Players Association deepens Silver\u2019s conviction that player-referee dealings ultimately are about relationships and professional respect. \u201cI feel pretty good going into the playoffs that we\u2019ve made some real progress there,\u201d Silver said. In other business at the spring BOG meetings: \u201cWe recognize that our goal was to put the best competition on the floor, and it\u2019s balanced against legitimate rebuilding of some teams,\u201d he said. \u201cBut I know we\u2019re not there yet, and I certainly wasn\u2019t satisfied.\u201d Changes in the lottery beginning in 2019 will flatten the odds and broaden the number of teams vying for the top picks, and Silver is hoping that improves the situation. \u201cThere can only be so much cajoling out of the league office,\u201d he said. \u201cIt\u2019s one of those things that the last place I want to go as the commissioner of the league office is to start dictating minutes and which particular players should be playing at what points in the game. \u201cI recognize that the incentives are not aligned right now. \u2026 My sense is we\u2019re still going to have some work to do.\u201d Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. In that game, the Celtics\u2019 lead had shriveled to 110-109. Philadelphia regained possession with five seconds left after an inbounds pass attempt by Boston\u2019s Bill Russell hit one of the wires that ran down from the ceiling of Boston Garden and helped support the baskets in those days. Hall of Fame guard Hal Greer prepared to toss the ball inbounds under his own basket. The logical target seemed to be massive Wilt Chamberlain in the low post, but Russell fronted Chamberlain and took away that option. K.C. Jones, guarding Greer, leaped along the baseline and frantically waved his arms to distract him as the five seconds ticked away. Havlicek had taken a position several feet off the direct line between Greer and Chet Walker, making it look like Walker was open when he really wasn\u2019t. After counting off a couple of seconds in his head, Havlicek sneaked a peek over his shoulder at Greer just as he prepared to release the ball. Havlicek tipped the inbounds pass to teammate Sam Jones, who dribbled out the clock to secure the win as fans poured onto the court. The Celtics had the win, and would go on to capture their seventh consecutive championship. (AP) \u2014 And now the real season begins for the Los Angeles Lakers. At least that\u2019s what the fans of the 16-time NBA champions repeatedly have been told while Los Angeles missed the playoffs and finished with a losing record for the fifth consecutive year. Ever since they took over the Lakers\u2019 basketball operations a year ago, Magic Johnson and general manager Rob Pelinka have founded their rebuilding project around the acquisition of one or two elite free agents this summer \u2013 or next summer, they\u2019ve recently amended. Magic firmly believes the Lakers\u2019 championship pedigree, glamorous location and salary cap room for two enormous contracts can attract superstars near the primes of their careers to lift one of the NBA\u2019s cornerstone franchises back to its accustomed heights. The Lakers had only missed the playoffs five previous times in their first 65 years of existence before they doubled that number during this dismal half-decade. But while the pursuit of Paul George and LeBron James constantly loomed on the horizon, the Lakers\u2019 day-to-day development over the past season has been downright intriguing. For all their talk of the Lakers\u2019 irresistible lure, Johnson and Pelinka knew they probably couldn\u2019t attract free-agent stars without an attractive young core, and the last six months suggested they have it. \u201cIf guys want to come here, they come,\u201d rookie Kyle Kuzma said Thursday after his exit interview with team brass. \u201cBut if not, we\u2019re not depending on that. We just think about, `How can we make the team better?\u201d\u2018 Rookies Lonzo Ball, Kuzma and Josh Hart all showed clear NBA ability, while youngsters Brandon Ingram and Julius Randle continued their development into above-average pros. Los Angeles\u2019 35-47 record and 11th-place conference finish in the just-completed season are its best performances by a considerable margin during this woeful five-year stretch. The Lakers aren\u2019t good yet, but the guys who are already in purple and gold could be key pieces in a very good team. Here are more things to watch during another long offseason in LA: PG SWEEPSTAKES: The Lakers are expected to put a full-court press on George, the Oklahoma City Thunder star who grew up idolizing Kobe Bryant in Palmdale, just north of Los Angeles. Even if James decides to stay home in Cleveland instead of becoming a dual-threat Hollywood mogul, the addition of George\u2019s 20-plus points per night almost certainly would make the Lakers very entertaining in coach Luke Walton\u2019s up-tempo system this fall. If George decides to play for a franchise closer to a championship, the Lakers will undoubtedly spin the narrative to the summer of 2019, when another bumper crop of free agents will be on the market. RANDLE\u2019S FUTURE: Randle\u2019s improvement was among the Lakers\u2019 most impressive developments of the season. The formerly inconsistent power forward played in all 82 games, averaging 16.1 points and 8.0 rebounds and emerging as a dependable two-way player. Randle is a free agent this summer after the Lakers decided not to lock him down with a contract extension last year, but Randle\u2019s outstanding year has greatly increased the chances of a long-term deal in LA. \u201cAs far as the summer, who knows what will happen?\u201d Randle said. \u201cI\u2019ve never done this before. I think it\u2019s exciting for me, though.\u201d FREE AGENCY: The Lakers\u2019 desire for plenty of cap room this summer means they\u2019ve got four talented veterans with expiring contracts: Brook Lopez, Kentavious Caldwell-Pope, Isaiah Thomas and Channing Frye. The other spot could go to veteran shooter Kyle Korver, who has been slowed by a foot injury and whose status remains uncertain \u201cHe\u2019s getting better,\u201d Lue said of Korver, who also missed time recently following his brother\u2019s death. \u201cI\u2019m not sure if he\u2019s 100 percent yet, but he\u2019s getting better and that\u2019s all you can ask for.\u201d If Korver isn\u2019t ready, Rodney Hood, one of four players acquired at the trade deadline, will likely start. He\u2019s been slowed by a sore left Achilles, but Lue said Hood\u2019s \u201cready to go.\u201d As for Cleveland\u2019s reserves, Jordan Clarkson, J.R. Smith and Jose Calderon are in the rotational mix, with forwards Tristan Thompson and Larry Nance Jr. being used depending on whom Indiana has on the floor. One of the holdovers from Cleveland\u2019s past three Finals teams, Thompson played extended minutes in the second half of Wednesday\u2019s loss to New York while Nance rested, a sign that Lue might be leaning toward him playing more. Thompson was excused from Thursday\u2019s practice because of the birth of his daughter. Lue trusts all his players. He\u2019s had no choice following a turbulent regular season in which he didn\u2019t always know who would be available until moments before tipoff. But Lue has depth and intends to use it. Hall of Fame coach Pat Riley\u2019s philosophy in the playoffs was: \u201cYou rotate eight players. You play seven. You use six and you trust five.\u201d Not Lue. Not this year. \u201cYou usually go nine,\u201d he said. \u201cI think we\u2019ve been nine in the past, but I think it\u2019s going to be important to go 10 for us because I like Clarkson being on the floor with a point guard, so that makes us play 10.\u201d Hill, who has plenty of playoff experience with San Antonio, Indiana and Utah, said Cleveland\u2019s versatility may give the Cavs an advantage. \u201cI\u2019ve been on a team that played a 10-man rotation in the playoffs, but I haven\u2019t been on a team this deep, so I think we\u2019ll have a lot of great guys that we can throw out there on the court with big lineups, small lineups,\u201d he said. \u201cI think we can play any type of style.\u201d For Hill, the matchup against the Pacers represents a homecoming, as he played for IUPUI and spent five years with the Pacers. It\u2019s also a chance to win a championship with James, a former playoff nemesis. Hill played on those Pacers teams that were eliminated in three straight postseasons by James and the Miami Heat. That can\u2019t happen this time. \u201cThe good thing is we got the best player in the world playing for us,\u201d Hill said. \u201cSo I\u2019m very excited about that. I don\u2019t got to get put out by him this year, which I did a lot in my career. This prelude is better than what Moses Malone and Rudy Tomjanovich did. Better than the Towers of Ralph Sampson and rookie Akeem Olajuwon. Better than Hakeem Olajuwon (spelling change) and Big Shot Rob (aka Robert Horry) and Kenny \u201cThe Jet\u201d Smith managed. Better than Olajuwon, Clyde Drexler and Sir Charles. Better than Tracy McGrady and Yao Ming. This team just won a league-and franchise-high 65 games , claimed the 2017-18 regular-season title and home-court advantage for the Western Conference playoffs. And most refreshing of all, emerged mostly free of battle scars from the grind ( get well soon, Luc Mbah a Moute ). This team boasts the strong Kia MVP favorite in James Harden, who could win the award over LeBron James and get little to no blowback from the basketball public if he does. They have Mike D\u2019Antoni, the Professor of Offensive Basketball and reigning Coach of the Year who followed up 2016-17 with a season just as satisfying as that one \u2014 if not more. They bring nine-time All-Star point guard Chris Paul, too. The welcome newcomer had a static-free seven-month bond with Harden and, therefore, threw a javelin through the notion that ball-dominant players and strong personalities can\u2019t share the court, too. The Rockets have pretty much done it all since October, with buckets of 3-pointers , a top-10 defense and a rumble through the NBA that left a trail of scattered bodies (including one in particular from the Bay Area). It was, says D\u2019Antoni, \u201ca special season\u201d that checked all the boxes and set the franchise record for wins and chest-bumps. This was cleanly executed, almost flawlessly performed and noticeably free of setbacks and drama. Yet Harden, speaking for everyone involved, could only summarize it with an obvious observation: \u201cWe haven\u2019t done anything yet.\u201d But of course. The optimism in Houston generated from where the Rockets sit at the table of championship contenders is served with a side dish of hesitation. There is nothing but confidence buzzing inside the locker room \u2026 and a degree of skepticism outside of it. One reason is because the defending-champion Golden State Warriors are still standing in the way, keeping Houston\u2019s dynasty as a work in progress. The other? That would be the playoff ghosts of Paul, of Harden and of D\u2019Antoni, the three most important members of this Rockets run who must distance themselves from previous springtime failures \u2014 or become buried by them. A championship for those three wouldn\u2019t quite have the reverberation LeBron James\u2019 win for Cleveland in 2016 did or even what the Warriors\u2019 title in 2015 did for the Bay Area after a 40-year NBA title drought. But it would have its own special place for three people who\u2019ve helped shape the NBA we see today. Maybe it\u2019s no coincidence, then, that the Basketball Gods put them on the same team and essentially formed the league\u2019s most obvious support group. But don\u2019t expect them to sit around a circle and swap what-if tales. When you raise the subject with Paul, Harden and D\u2019Antoni \u2014 here on the eve of this, their best shot at a title \u2014 they refuse to make a big deal out of what\u2019s undoubtedly a Big Deal. Do they accept accountability for the past? Yes, although in each case, circumstances beyond their control dictated quick playoff exits. Do they dwell on it? Not in public anyway, although a title is clearly the missing piece of their respective legacies. And, your hunch says, an irritating (if not painful) void. \u201cWe know it would mean everything to them,\u201d said Rockets forward and resident tough-guy PJ Tucker. \u201cI mean, they deserve one. Probably should have one by now.\u201d Maybe that changes soon, especially for Paul. Nobody has played in more playoff games (76) and without reaching the conference finals. It is easily the scarlet letter in the career of a future Hall of Famer. With Harden, Paul is blessed with the only NBA top-five teammate he\u2019s ever had (sorry, Blake Griffin). In D\u2019Antoni, he has a coach who\u2019s receptive to suggestions and has an overall better shot at beating the mighty Warriors. Both are things Paul couldn\u2019t manage on the LA Clippers when facing the Steve Kerr-era Warriors. Only once, however, did Paul face-splat in the playoffs. That came in late in Game 5 of the 2014 conference semifinals against the Thunder, as his Clippers blew a big lead and a chance to go up 3-2. Oklahoma City would close out the series in Los Angeles. In the 2015 West semis, the Clippers blew a 3-1 series lead to the Rockets , but Paul was mostly superb in that series. In fact, his playoff averages ( 21.4 points, 9.4 assists, 38.1 percent on 3-pointers ) are in line with his season averages. After that crushing loss to the Rockets came an avalanche of crummy luck. Paul and Griffin suffered injuries that either limited them or knocked them out of the playoffs . With limited scoring options sans Paul and Griffin, the Clippers had no chance. Still, no team that won 60 percent of its games over a six-season stretch failed to make the conference finals \u2026 except the Paul-led Clippers. Paul\u2019s arrival in Houston runs somewhat parallel to Drexler\u2019s back in 1995 . The Hall of Famer spent a 11 1\/2 seasons with the Blazers without winning a title, although he did reach two Finals ( 1990 , \u201992 ). His championship moment didn\u2019t come until he joined Olajuwon and raised the trophy. Drexler, now a TV analyst for the Rockets, feels a kinship with Paul\u2019s path. \u201cI said it 30 years ago, it\u2019s a team sport not an individual sport, and you can have 50 points and 30 rebounds but if I miss two free throws at the end of the game, we don\u2019t win,\u201d Drexler said. \u201cIt\u2019s always nice to be on a team that\u2019s good enough and lucky enough and healthy enough but it doesn\u2019t always happen. \u201cHe\u2019s phenomenally skilled, highly talented, one of the best to ever play the game. He\u2019s tremendous to James, who has another guy he respects and can depend on. Chris can give him a rest off the ball and when that happens we don\u2019t lose the lead; the lead goes up.\u201d After Paul nudged the Clippers to trade him to Houston last summer, Paul made a point to forge a relationship with Harden right away, within days. That was critical as Paul wanted to let Harden know he wasn\u2019t coming to overwhelm Harden, only to help him. It\u2019s no coincidence that Paul\u2019s popular State Farm commercials seized the chance to add Harden this year . \u201cAbsolutely,\u201d said Paul, when asked about the importance of that time together. \u201cThis all started this summer when we spent some time together. We got a tight knit group because of that. And what I\u2019ve learned is James wants this as bad as anyone. His love for the game is strong. He loves to play just as much as anybody I\u2019ve been around. You don\u2019t know about these things until you\u2019re with somebody for a while.\u201d It might be an advantage for Paul in that, probably for the first time in his life, he\u2019s not the most important player on the team. Once this year\u2019s votes are added up, Harden is expected to collect more MVP love than anyone except LeBron. \u201cThere\u2019s no egos,\u201d said Paul, explaining why this has worked. \u201cEverything is collaborative.\u201d The ball is mostly in Harden\u2019s hands, which makes sense, given his ability to create for himself and others. He finished as the league\u2019s scoring champ with 30.4 ppg and ranked third in assists (8.8 apg) , behind only James and Rajon Rondo. Both stats are reflective of Harden\u2019s talent and Paul\u2019s willingness to play the co-star. Harden\u2019s playoff ghosts aren\u2019t as sinister as Paul\u2019s as he has reached the conference finals three times and, as a member of the Thunder, made it to The Finals in 2012 . Yet he evaporated in that championship series, and no-showed in Game 6 of the 2017 West semis when the Rockets were eliminated by the Spurs . Harden had gaudy playoff numbers in the last three postseasons ( 27.5 ppg, 7.8 rpg, 5.5 apg ) without the benefit of a reliable No. 2 guy to absorb some of the heat. Therefore, he sounds relieved, more than anything, when discussing his chances with Paul. \u201cIt\u2019s just his passion, how much he cares, how much he studies,\u201d said Harden. \u201cIt shows up on the court. And if I\u2019m at home and if it\u2019s an off night, he\u2019ll call me and say, \u2018Hey, did you see the game, are you watching the game?\u2019 He loves basketball, and that gives you that extra drive because you know he\u2019s tuned in. Here\u2019s a guy, that\u2019s 13 years in the league, and he still has the enthusiasm like he\u2019s a rookie. He brings so much to this team and obviously to myself. Every day I learn something from him.\u201d Of all coaches, D\u2019Antoni might be the one who has influenced much of what we see from today\u2019s game: torrid offensive pace, an infatuation with the 3-pointer and a spread floor. Before he took over the job and the Rockets began breaking NBA records for 3-point shooting and scoring , he oversaw the Phoenix Suns and their seven-seconds-or-less approach to shooting. It became revolutionary and although those teams were entertaining as heck (and rolled up the score), they produced zero championships. He is 32-38 lifetime in the postseason. That of course led to a chorus about D\u2019Antoni\u2019s basketball-on-steriods system and how it was hamstrung by the slower, defensive-minded pace of the playoffs. This ignored the reality that the Suns then were no match in the playoffs for Dirk Nowitzki\u2019s Dallas Mavericks and Tim Duncan\u2019s San Antonio Spurs (and later Kobe Bryant\u2019s Los Angeles Lakers), no matter how many scoreboards they broke. Also, the offense the Rockets employ is an almost completely different approach to basketball than those Suns\u2019 teams (or the Linsanity teams D\u2019Antoni coached in New York). Phoenix didn\u2019t rely nearly as much on the long ball as they used All-Stars Steve Nash and Amar\u2019e Stoudemire to pick-and-roll folks to a slow death. And the Rockets don\u2019t fast break like those Phoenix teams. If anything, D\u2019Antoni has proved masterful in creating a system that best exploits his team\u2019s strengths, and this year had to tweak it to embrace what Paul brings. He says, with some modesty: \u201cYou go with the skill level of the players, which has gotten so much better lately. The 3-point shot has done that. If you can\u2019t shoot 3s, you better be really good at other things. The 3 has changed the way players have looked at the game, how coaches coach the game.\u201d His system has perhaps inflated the profile of role players such as Ryan Anderson, Eric Gordon and Trevor Ariza. They have a combined zero All-Star appearances but often deliver massive games, thanks partly to the number of open looks at the basket. D\u2019Antoni adds: \u201cYou just try to get the most out of what you got. Everybody\u2019s trying to put a label on things. Sometimes you can\u2019t. The player dictates how they play and we\u2019re trying to allow them to be the best version of themselves. I will say this is the best team I\u2019ve been around. Never had a team this deep.\u201d As for the Rockets\u2019 chances of getting hardware with this approach, D\u2019Antoni doesn\u2019t grit his teeth as much anymore about the question. Rather, he says, why not? \u201cThere\u2019s not a concrete reason of, \u2018Yeah, but it was in the regular season.\u2019 No, it\u2019s all the same,\u201d he said. \u201cSo, there\u2019s no reason why we can\u2019t. We just haven\u2019t proven it. But we haven\u2019t had this team to be able to prove it. Obviously, the playoffs are a different animal. It\u2019s exciting more than anything. Definitely not a worry.\u201d Some Rockets were privately annoyed when Kerr said the Warriors could beat anyone without Stephen Curry ( who finished the season injured ). They also wondered why Golden State still doesn\u2019t have as much respect for the Rockets as they should, even after losing twice to Houston this season. Without a doubt, the Warriors are in the Rockets\u2019 heads, as they should be, since no other team in the West lives in the same area code as these two. \u201cThey know about us,\u201d Gordon said. \u201cThey know. There\u2019s a reason we have the best record. As long as we play together on offense and on defense as we have been, I\u2019ll take out chances against anybody.\u201d Paul said he will enter the playoffs more confident about this team\u2019s chances than any he\u2019s ever been on before, which says plenty, considering the Clippers had three All-Stars in their prime. \u201cWe got so many different weapons,\u201d Paul said, \u201cso many different ways to play. The spirit of our team has just been right.\u201d Harden adds: \u201cThe groundwork that we put in from last year, the additions this year, it\u2019s put us where we want to be.\u201d During the summer together prepping for this season, Harden and Paul envisioned a 2017-18 where they would get the best record in the NBA, grab home court for the playoffs and, in June, finally discover what the Larry O\u2019Brien trophy felt like. They\u2019re two-thirds there to a finish line that always managed to extend just beyond their reach. Are the Rockets the biggest threat to the Warriors? Or a bigger one to themselves? Harden claims a clear sense of what the immediate future will bring for himself, D\u2019Antoni, Paul and the Rockets, and with the regular season they just had, he is expecting to witness this: \u201cGreatness,\u201d he said. * * * Veteran NBA writer Shaun Powell has worked for newspapers and other publications for more than 25 years. And come July, when free agency and Summer League action do their choreographed dance, dreams starting taking a much more realistic shape for lottery squads. Culture. That\u2019s what Nets coach Kenny Atkinson is working on building in Brooklyn. That, plus a player-development program that has seen the likes of Spencer Dinwiddie, Rondae Hollis-Jefferson and Caris LeVert develop into productive players. Doing so cost former GM Rich Cho his job and provides his replacement, Mitch Kupchak , a prime opportunity to show what he learned about championship-level team-building during his years with the Los Angeles Lakers. Charlotte certainly worked for veteran big man Dwight Howard, who had a career resurgence in 2017-18 . All-Star guard Kemba Walker survived trade rumors all season and remains one of the league\u2019s most dynamic backcourt talents. The rebuild from rock bottom will go a lot faster if the Bulls put their trust in Zach LaVine and Lauri Markannen as the headliners for the future. That gives rookie Dennis Smith Jr., the future face of the franchise, another season to learn from the all-time face of the franchise. Now that the \u201cLob City\u201d era is officially over, the Clippers get to chart a new course with all of the framework in place for a quick turnaround. The best sixth man in the business (Lou Williams) and a former All-Star big man (DeAndre Jordan) headline the holdovers. Isaiah Thomas is one the mend (again) and wants in on the party, too. And now, the best time of the NBA year is finally here. \u2022 Complete 2018 NBA Playoff Bracket The playoffs are back, with Golden State and Cleveland \u2014 who\u2019ve met in the last three NBA Finals \u2014 not exactly looking like the overwhelming favorites the league has come to expect. A fourth straight Warriors-Cavs matchup is no guarantee. A fourth straight trip by either of those teams alone is far from certain, either. Out West, it\u2019s No. 1 Houston meeting No. 8 Minnesota \u2013 which earned its berth by topping Denver in the NBA\u2019s first win-and-get-in, lose-and-go-home regular-season finale in 21 years. No. 2 Golden State opens its quest for a third championship in four seasons against No. 7 San Antonio, No. 3 Portland drew No. 6 New Orleans and No. 4 Oklahoma City opens against No. 5 Utah. \u201cThe playoffs are about moments, and you just want a chance to have those moments,\u201d Miami guard Dwyane Wade said. It\u2019s Minnesota\u2019s first berth since 2004, and ended what was the NBA\u2019s longest playoff drought (which now is bequeathed to the Sacramento Kings, who last played a playoff game in 2006). \u201cThey just made the plays at the end,\u201d Denver\u2019s Will Barton said of the Timberwolves. \u201cGive them credit.\u201d There are some rematches from the 2017 playoffs already waiting. Cleveland swept Indiana in the first round last year, and Boston needed seven games to oust Washington in a thrilling East semifinal series. Those two teams played in the West finals last season, and the Spurs were up by 23 points when Leonard left in the third quarter after getting injured on a play where he tried a jumper and Warriors center Zaza Pachulia closed out aggressively. Leonard got hurt when he stepped on Pachulia on the landing; he and the Spurs haven\u2019t been the same since. Leonard never returned to that series, and played in only nine games for the Spurs this season. \u201cThere is no pressure on us,\u201d Spurs star LaMarcus Aldridge said. There is on Golden State. The Warriors are trying for a third title in four seasons, and will play this first round more than likely without Curry while he continues recovering from a knee injury. Golden State went 7-10 in its final 17 games, and clearly sputtered across the finish line.
